"},{"id":41256,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/nba-usa-basketball-youth-basektball-rules","date":"2018-03-20","title":"NBA, USA Basketball announce age-appropriate rules and standards for youth basketball competitions","content":"What height should the basket be set at for a game being played by eight-year-old boys or girls? What size ball should they be using? Should there be a 3-point line? Should they play man-to-man or zone defense? Should there be a shot clock? These are all questions posed by parents, coaches and youth basketball organizations throughout the country. But there have never been universal answers to those questions as different organizations often played by different rules. A child could play on a nine-foot rim with a smaller ball in one league, then have to play on a 10-foot rim with a larger ball in another. That lack of uniformity in youth basketball development led the National Basketball Association and USA Basketball to come together to establish guidelines to promote health and wellness, skill development and enjoyment of the game for boys and girls of all different age ranges. \u201cOur sport has lacked governance at the youth level so we came together really to establish what we think are a series of best practices for how to play the game at the youth level,\u201d said David Krichavsky, Vice President of Youth Basketball Development at the NBA. After announcing the Health and Wellness guidelines in Oct. 2016, the two organizations are ready to implement next step in their quest to improve the state of youth basketball in this country. That step is today\u2019s announcement of a set of age-appropriate rules and standards for youth basketball competitions, which includes recommendations surrounding equipment, court specifications, game structure, playing tactics and rules of the game. \u201cWhat we tried to do is address questions that we would get all the time, whether it be from individual parents or the commissioners or coaches,\u201d said Krichavsky. \u201cWe can answer all of those questions on a one-off basis, but now we have a set of standards that we can point everyone to and a set of standards that was developed by experts in our game and experts on youth development. So it will create a level of consistency that never existed before.\u201d The goal of these new guidelines is to establish the proper setting to help young players learn the fundamentals of the game, build their skills, achieve early success and provide long-term development opportunities. By using the Youth Basketball Player Segmentation Model, the rules and standards are specific to four age groups: ages 7-8, ages 9-1, ages 12-4 and grades 9-12. As players grow in their development, the guidelines adjust with them to keep them on track for success, growth and enjoyment. \u201cWe\u2019re excited to get these out and think it\u2019s well overdue here in this country,\u201d said Jim Tooley, the Chief Executive Officer of USA Basketball. \u201cWe\u2019re the only country really that plays by multiple sets of rules compared to other parts of the world where everyone is pretty much playing FIBA standard rules across the board.\u201d Highlights from the new rules and standards include the recommended use of: The complete guidelines can be found at: https:\/\/youthguidelines.nba.com\/ \u201cAs the chairman of both USA Basketball and the Jr. NBA Leadership Council, as well as a parent whose kids grew up playing and loving the game of basketball, I\u2019m proud that the NBA and USA Basketball are working together to develop and share these important guidelines, \u201c said Martin E. Dempsey, USA Basketball Chairman and Jr. NBA Leadership Council Chairman, Retired General. \u201cThe new guidelines include age-specific rules and standards that will ensure a balanced and positive basketball experience for youth of all ages.\u201d In terms of equal playing time, the key is helping coaches find the right balance between participation and competition. \u201cA key point here is that you never know whether a young boy or young girl is going to evolve into being a great player,\u201d said Tooley. \u201cThis is designed for all kids; a lot of these kids won\u2019t make their high school team let alone go to college on a D-I scholarship and end up in the NBA or WNBA. This is designed for them to have success on their own pathway and to have success you have to be a part of it, you have to participate, you have to play.\u201d When it comes to the 3-point shot, Tooley recalls conversations he\u2019s had with a friend who coaches a group of nine-year-olds that are all eager to imitate Stephen Curry by launching 3-pointers from 25-plus feet from the basket. Krichavsky has seen it plenty of times as well. \u201cI think we\u2019ve all seen those examples of kids moving beyond the arc and shooting from their hips and really shooting with technique that is really not the appropriate technique,\u201d Krichavsky said. \u201cSo if we take that line away and teach kids proper form and reinforce that. Certainly we can bring that line back \u2013 and in our guidelines we do that \u2013 but it gives the opportunity to learn proper shooting form and reinforce that from appropriate ranges and then continue to have them step back. But keep that proper shooting form as they do so.\u201d Perhaps the most difficult practices to adopt will be the ones involving new equipment. Not many youth organizations currently have shot clocks. Not everyone has baskets that are adjustable to different heights to accommodate different age groups. While it may take time for all of these guidelines to be implemented across the board, both the NBA and USA Basketball have upcoming events that will highlight these new rules and standards. \u201cOur working group was pretty diverse; we had folks from the NCAA, high schools and AAU involved [in establishing these guidelines] so we\u2019re hopeful that will help spread these rules and standards,\u201d said Tooley. \u201cAnd both the NBA through their Jr. NBA World Championship and USA Basketball with our U.S. Open Basketball Championships, we have our universe and touch points with a lot of youth basketball organizations that is much bigger than its ever been. So we\u2019re going to be adopting these rules and standards along the way and we just hope it becomes adopted over time.\u201d With the guidelines available for everyone to learn and implement as well as seeing examples of those guidelines in action at the Jr. NBA World Championship and U.S. Open Basketball Championships this spring and summer, what are the next steps for the NBA and USA Basketball in their continued quest to improve youth basketball? \u201cWe\u2019re going to watch and listen, get feedback and see what we\u2019re hearing out there and respond accordingly,\u201d said Tooley. \u201cBut we feel pretty bullish that these are really solid principles to go by and both of our respective tournaments that we\u2019ll be having will yield a lot of influence in this space as well. And I know both the Jr. NBA and USA Basketball are excited to get those up and running this summer.\u201d"},{"id":41254,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/golden-state-warriors-report-stephen-curry-targets-friday-return","date":"2018-03-20","title":"Golden State Warriors star Stephen Curry cleared to participate in full team practices","content":"After losing their fourth All-Star to injury on Monday night, the Golden State Warriors may get at least one back in the mix later this week. "},{"id":41255,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/cleveland-cavaliers-tyronn-lue-unique-challenge-lebron-james","date":"2018-03-19","title":"Pressure to win creates stressful job for Cleveland Cavaliers coach Tyronn Lue","content":" You don\u2019t need to have hooped at UMass or taken flight from the foul line to be \u201cDoctor\u201d enough to know Tyronn Lue was due for a change.  I locked eyes with the Cleveland Cavaliers coach as he exited United Center Saturday night, having stayed in the locker room for the second half of his team\u2019s game against the Chicago Bulls with unspecified \u201cillness\u201d issues. With a vulnerable, clammy and weary expression on his face, he looked like a man who needed to do exactly what he did Monday \u2013 take a leave of absence from his Cavs duties to get his priorities, notably his health, straight. Lue, 40, announced his decision to temporarily step away from the white board in a statement released by the Cavaliers . \u201cI have had chest pains and other troubling symptoms, compounded by a loss of sleep, throughout the year,\u201d he said. \u201cDespite a battery of tests, there have been no conclusions as to what the exact issue is.\u201d Initial reports were that Lue would sit out a week before rejoining the team, although when Charlotte\u2019s Steve Clifford similarly tapped out early this season to put his health first, he was gone for six weeks after being diagnosed with severe sleep deprivation. It\u2019s been said that most NBA coaches sleep like babies: a few hours here and there, interrupted by fits of wailing. Neither Lue nor Cleveland general manager Koby Altman, in his supportive comment (\u201cWe know how difficult these circumstances are,\u201d Altman said) used the word \u201cstress\u201d in assessing Lue\u2019s condition. But they could have, because stress is the elephant in almost every coach\u2019s room. That\u2019s no \u201cEureka!\u201d diagnosis, by the way. The trick is understanding that stress can manifest itself in as many ways as it\u2019s generated. Among the obvious ones, there is stress to win. Then, having won, there is stress to keep winning. Stress from lofty expectations and sustained excellence, which both the Cavaliers and the Golden State Warriors are facing at unprecedented levels by NBA standards as they try to line up for a fourth consecutive Finals clash. Stress comes, too, from falling short of such expectations. As well as nosediving into a losing streak, making a dumb mistake or, deep in one\u2019s gut, being exposed as unworthy altogether. There\u2019s stress when the team flight leaves late due to a weather or mechanical issue. Stress over a digital scouting tool that doesn\u2019t fire up precisely when needed. Stress to manage your players\u2019 minutes, develop that raw young guy or heed a rehabber\u2019s injury restrictions. Always, there\u2019s the original and inevitable stress to beat the other team, whose coach may be as stressed as you. It would be na\u00efve in considering Lue\u2019s case not to explore the stress of coaching LeBron James. Just as there are special opportunities and privileges that come with having the best player in basketball on your side, there is duty, obligation and stress to working with him, too. Specifically, that means maximizing his talents and experience to the beat of a ticking clock that represents his useful remaining court life or contract term, against the backdrop of the superstar\u2019s own agenda. James burns to win \u2013 it\u2019s his raison d\u2019\u00eatre for pushing toward 82 appearances this season, for delving deep into personal training methods to stay resilient and available at age 33. His high basketball IQ, lauded by so many (including James), means he functions as half-a-coach most of the time, with the sway he holds over every organization for which he plays suggesting the other half at times. James\u2019 personality, frequently described as passive-aggressive, keeps pressure on all around him, whether it\u2019s a current or former teammate like Kyrie Irving to fill and accept the role James sized up for him, a GM like Altman to make roster moves at the trade deadline under an implicit or-else, or an owner like Dan Gilbert to spend extravagantly into a payroll spill of luxury-tax red in hopes of fulfilling James\u2019 ambitions. No one, however, shoulders more pressure to keep up with James \u2013 and the stress that can comes with it \u2013 than his head coaches. On game nights or on practice days, drawing up Xs & Os, knowing how hard to scrimmage or giving the squad a day off entirely. As vocal as James was in supporting Lue when he took over for former coach David Blatt midway through the Cavs\u2019 2015-16 championship season, and as understanding as he sounded in talking about the coach\u2019s health Saturday night and sudden sabbatical Monday \u2013 \u201cLike losing one of your best players,\u201d James said, calling Lue \u201cthe captain of the ship\u201d \u2013 the four-time MVP sure likely has added to Lue\u2019s predicament. With his presence, with with his capabilities and, yes, with his manner too. Before Lue took ill Saturday, conversations I had around United Center with several longtime NBA insiders centered on the challenge of coaching James for his personality, not his athletic gifts. Specifically, I sought their reactions to the sideline scene captured on cameras in Portland Thursday when James barked at Lue on Cleveland\u2019s bench and Lue felt compelled to bark back. The score had gone south. The Cavs were on No. 5 of a six-game, 11-day trip. They were headed to a 15-20 record since their high-water mark of 24-9 on Dec. 21 eight weeks earlier. They essentially have been three teams in one this season: Pre-trade, post-trade and injury-riddled. So when James and Lue started up, the players and assistant coaches in between looked like kids staring hard at the TV while their parents argued. Lue dismissed it afterward as a miscommunication over James\u2019 desire to be subbed out sooner. A couple of folks attributed it to disagreement over shot selection, as in putting the ball on the floor vs. settling for a jumper. No one hinted it reached any sort of \u201cI hate you! Yeah, well I hate you too!\u201d irreconcilable level, though a few guessers and snarkers on social media dusted off the \u201cThere goes Ty Lue yelling at the Cavs\u2019 coach again\u201d memes. But the optics. Oh, the optics. Having that sort of exchange \u2013 urgent, heated, even hypothetically personal \u2013 away from prying eyes is no biggie in sports. But James has had them repeatedly with just about every coach for whom he has played. From disagreement with Paul Silas, who coached him his first two seasons, to the shoulder shiver he had for Miami\u2019s Erik Spoelstra in their first season together. From shoving Blatt aside to keep the coach from getting a technical foul to vetoing his last-second playoff strategy against Chicago in 2015. From the timeout huddle James animatedly took over against Toronto in January, his voice and gestures drowning out the staff\u2019s, all the way to last week\u2019s mini-drama at the Moda Center. Some qualifiers are in order here: Thirty NBA coaches would welcome James on their roster. Most would appreciate his input, however vocal and passionate, based on his knowledge not just of his skills but those of the other nine guys on the floor at any given moment. As for anyone who doesn\u2019t accept the expectations and pressure that come with coaching him \u2013 the stress \u2013 well, there are a bunch of comfortable Division III or G League jobs where the spotlight doesn\u2019t burn at all. It\u2019s equally fair to say, though, that James has had more coach commotions captured and archived than any elite player in NBA history. Some of that is a by-product of performing in this 24\/7 media age. Some of it undoubtedly is an outgrowth of James\u2019 status, alone perhaps in team sports, as a partner rather than just a player, a co-everything \u2013 coach, GM, owner \u2013 in impact and self-awareness beyond outrageously salaried employee. But some of it, too, is a style that\u2019s indulgent and chaneable. Such that even NBA insiders, Cavs personnel or scouts were left struggling to defend it or shaking their heads. \u201cLook, they were playing cards on the flight from Portland the day after,\u201d one said. \u201cBut would it be better to do that [arguing] behind closed doors? Yes it would.\u201d Said another, after Lue stayed in the locker room for Saturday\u2019s second half: \u201cMaybe he just decided, \u2018I\u2019ve tried. Somebody else try.\u2019\u201d \u201cWhen a coach gets called out by a star,\u201d said a third, \u201che has to go back at him. Because the other players are watching.\u201d Maybe Lue\u2019s need to attend to his health is wholly physical, something to be remedied with melatonin and a more consistent bedtime. Maybe it\u2019s the equivalent of Dickens\u2019 undigested bit of beef, blot of mustard or fragment of underdone potato, which was what Scrooge was rooting for before he got his reset. Or maybe it stems in some part from the power dynamic unique to the Cavaliers, largely created and certainly embraced by James, mostly for the organization\u2019s good but not without some unintended consequences. * * * Steve Aschburner has written about the NBA since 1980. Allen has written a soon-to-hit-the-stores book, \u201cFrom The Outside: My Journey Through Life and the Game I Love,\u201d and mentions Rondo several times, not necessarily in the most flattering ways. If you notice something different during the upcoming playoffs, it\u2019s probably all the guys playing meaningful postseason roles for their teams who \u2026 never saw Michael Jordan play meaningful postseason games. (Pause here to pour one out for myself.) Rarely have the playoffs approached with so many teams being so dependent on young guys contributing in meaningful roles. Almost every team currently in the mix has multiple guys who\u2019ve never or barely played in the postseason in their current rotations. Barring injuries, they\u2019re likely to stay in those rotations when the playoffs begin. From Toronto (where rookie O.G. Anunoby has started most of the year at small forward, and whose bench is comprised of kid after kid \u2014 Fred VanVleet, Pascal Siakam, Jakob Poeltl and Delon Wright) to Boston (which has started Jaylen Brown at the two all season and Jayson Tatum at the three since Gordon Hayward\u2019s season-ending injury ) to Philly (with Joel Embiid and Ben Simmons and Robert Covington all starting a Blutarsky of total playoff games (as in, zero) between them ), kids are in the mix. * Playoff picture:  Chases tighten in East, West \u201cA lot is made about how they\u2019re going to perform in the playoffs,\u201d Raptors coach Dwane Casey said of his young guys. \u201cBut, again, I can\u2019t find that manual anywhere that says you have to shorten the rotation in the playoffs. It\u2019s written somewhere, \u2018cause everybody keeps asking the question. I must have missed that memo.\u201d In an era of $200 million max deals, you have to \u2014 have to \u2014 find three to five players that can contribute for (relatively) little money. Most of the time, that\u2019s going to be players on rookie deals, who teams can control for up to five years (and, not unimportantly, get some of their most productive seasons out of them during that stretch). Player development has been crucial in the league for pretty much the last decade, and with one-to-one G League affiliations for all 30 teams just around the corner it will become even more so. The Milwaukee Bucks\u2019 Giannis Antetokounmpo is a playoff graybeard compared to some others in the Eastern Conference \u2014 he has 12 whole postseason games: six in 2014-15, six last season \u2014 under his belt. The Bucks\u2019 starting point guard, Eric Bledsoe, has a whopping 17 playoff games to his credit \u2014 though none have come since 2013. The Indiana Pacers\u2019 Victor Oladipo got his first taste of the postseason last year in a supporting role to Russell Westbrook in Oklahoma City, and shot 34.4 percent \u2014 24 percent on 3-pointers \u2014 in the Thunder\u2019s five-game first-round loss to Houston . LeBron James will start his push for an eighth straight Finals appearance overall, and fourth in a row in Cleveland, depending on Larry Nance, Jr. (zero playoff appearances), Jordan Clarkson (zero playoff appearances) and Rodney Hood (11 playoff games total, all last season with the Utah Jazz) all now in the Cavaliers\u2019 rotation. Dwyane Wade has played in more playoff games \u2014 172 \u2014 than the bulk of his Miami Heat teammates combined . Thirteen of his 14 teammates have played a total of 141 playoff games, and 30 of those are by Goran Dragic. (Udonis Haslem, little-used this season at 37, has played in 147 career postseason games.) \u201cI think you just have to trust that the regular season, the battles throughout the regular season, seasoned guys enough to be prepared for that next stage,\u201d said Heat guard Wayne Elliington, who logged 14 postseason minutes in two games for Dallas in 2014. \u201cWe\u2019ve got a lot of young guys on our team, case in point. I\u2019ve seen guys have big games. They rise to the competition. Hopefully that\u2019s what it\u2019ll be about in the playoffs with us.\u201d There\u2019s more experience in the Western Conference, but even there, teams such as the Minnesota Timberwolves have key players like Karl-Anthony Towns and Andrew Wiggins who\u2019ve never been in a playoff game. The New Orleans Pelicans will be counting on Anthony Davis ( four playoff games total in his first five NBA seasons ) to lead them, when the officiating is different and the game plans are more detailed, with the best coaches in the world devising ways to stop you. Maybe I\u2019m overthinking this. \u201cIn 2008 when we won, we had (Kendrick) Perkins, (Rajon) Rondo, Baby (Glen Davis), and (Leon) Powe,\u201d said Boston Celtics general manager Danny Ainge. \u201cLate firsts and second-round picks. They all contributed a lot. It\u2019s nothing new. In managing the salary cap, you need rookie scale and or minimum contracts.\u201d Or, maybe I\u2019m not. Philadelphia 76ers coach Brett Brown noted that his young players, who handle the ball the most on his team, have to cut down on their turnovers going into the playoffs \u2014 a belief he reiterated over the weekend . In doing so he sounded an awful lot like Steve Kerr did when he took over the Golden State Warriors in 2014, when he lamented the \u201cplays of insanity\u201d by his young guards, primarily Stephen Curry, which produced unforced turnovers. Now, the fact is, someone\u2019s kids will play well, and come through, and be a big part of a long postseason run. But whose? \u201cFirst, you\u2019ve got to make it,\u201d said Timberwolves coach Tom Thibodeau, who has seen both sides of the playoff divide, having sat on Boston\u2019s bench when the Kevin Garnett\/Paul Pierce\/Ray Allen team of tough-minded vets won The Finals in 2008 , and then coaching a painfully young Derrick Rose, Joakim Noah and their teammates through a long playoff run two seasons later . \u201cIf you\u2019re fortunate enough to make it, you understand that there\u2019s another intensity level when you get to the playoffs,\u201d Thibodeau said. \u201cThis is what you play for. If you love competition, this brings out the best in you.\u201d Towns loves competition. But he hasn\u2019t experienced it for himself yet. \u201cIt\u2019s just a fight,\u201d he said. \u201cOne play throughout the game can change the whole course of it. It\u2019s a feeling of trying to be perfect. You\u2019ve got to be perfect. You have to go out there and try your best, but you can\u2019t make those simple mistakes you make in the regular season and be able to get away with it. It\u2019s a different philosophy \u2026 the playoffs is a different animal. The biggest thing in the playoffs is who\u2019s the first team to switch up their game plan.\u201d But nothing does like doing. \u201cI was nervous,\u201d said Washington Wizards All-Star guard Bradley Beal, whose first postseason series came in 2014 against Chicago . \u201cWe had Sam Cassell, and Witt (former Coach Randy Wittman), and Nene was crazy. The level of play, the fierceness, the competitive edge that everybody had, they treated every game like it was a win or lose situation. Both teams. Every time I\u2019ve been in the playoffs it\u2019s been that way. It\u2019s nothing like the regular season.\u201d Washington is one of the few East teams that will bring a core with no first-timers to the postseason. The Houston Rockets, Portland Trail Blazers, San Antonio Spurs and Warriors are similarly seeded throughout their rosters. But increasingly, those kinds of teams are the exception, not the rule. The Raptors may have the biggest postseason learning curve of any contender. Their young guys were in the lab all last summer \u2014 Siakam handling the ball, VanVleet and Wright working on their shooting. They have been crucial to Toronto\u2019s \u201cculture reset,\u201d as GM Masai Ujiri put it after his team was swept by Cleveland in the East semifinals last season . Their presence and production have cut the minutes of All-Stars Kyle Lowry and DeMar DeRozan, while also changing the iso style that the Raptors had maxed out at over the previous few years. None of them dominate the stat sheet \u2014 veteran C.J. Miles is tops off the bench in scoring, at 10.4 points per game in just under 19 minutes a night . But they all contribute. Some of Toronto\u2019s kids got a taste of things to come when they played on the team\u2019s G League team, Raptors 905, in the championship series last year, which it won over Rio Grande Valley in three games . \u201cIt was the first time for me being in a series situation; in college, it\u2019s one-and-done,\u201d said VanVleet, who got to the regional semis in the NCAA Tournament as a junior at Wichita State. \u201cI think I was thrown in there like, Game 2, of the three games of The Finals. Pascal played the whole playoffs. But just being thrown in a championship environment, it\u2019s big. It\u2019s not the same type of feel like a March Madness, but it\u2019s the same kind of intensity in terms of you want to get the job done in a playoff series. I think we were down 0-1 at the time, and try to come back and win the next two. It was just good experience getting under your belt, and you hope it can continue this year.\u201d Again, Miles is a big part of the Raptors\u2019 reserves. But VanVleet, Siakam, Poeltl and Wright have helped make Toronto\u2019s unit into one of the league most potent benches. They\u2019re first in the league in Defensive Rating (100.7) and steals per game (4.0) and third in made 3-pointers per game (4.9). When they\u2019re done, Lowry and DeRozan often are, too. \u201cIt was a crazy stretch, seven or eight games, where they didn\u2019t get to play the fourth quarter,\u201d Van Vleet said. \u201cThat was big for us, and for our team, keeping those guys fresh down the stretch. There\u2019s times where it\u2019ll be the second quarter. DeMar usually comes in with six or seven minutes left. It\u2019ll be all the way down to three or four before he checks back in. Those guys have been great, being unselfish, letting us kind of rock out when it\u2019s going good, and when it\u2019s not going good, all right, let\u2019s get back in there and settle this thing down. It\u2019s a good balance of both. But we\u2019ve got the utmost trust from our vets, and obviously from the coaching staff.\u201d The hard lessons the playoffs usually teach will be merciless. But those who survive it can often conquer it. Dennis Johnson went 0-for-14 in Game 7 of the 1978 Finals with the Seattle SuperSonics . The following year, he averaged 20.9 points and helped lead the Sonics to their only NBA title before repeating the feat twice more for Boston in \u201884 and \u201886. You only become a great playoff performer by getting there, and then getting back, again and again. \u201cI used to have a coach when I was younger who told me if you\u2019re not nervous in these types of situations, you\u2019re not ready to play,\u201d Beal said. \u201cFor me, it was getting my feet wet. After the first couple of minutes, you get used to it. It\u2019s like, okay, this is what it\u2019s about, this is what you have to do, this is how hard you have to play. And it just kind of takes off from there.\u201d * * * Longtime NBA reporter, columnist and Naismith Memorial Basketball Hall of Famer David Aldridge is an analyst for TNT. The views on this page do not necessarily reflect the views of the NBA, its clubs or Turner Broadcasting."},{"id":47342,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/morning-tip-next-moves-new-orleans-pelicans-after-owner-tom-benson-death","date":"2018-03-19","title":"Future of New Orleans Pelicans stable, even as team mourns owner Tom Benson's death","content":" How does the death of Tom Benson impact the future of the Pelicans in New Orleans? Benson, the longtime owner of the NFL\u2019s New Orleans Saints, died last week at 90 , leaving control of both the Saints and Pelicans, which he bought from the NBA in 2012, to his wife, Gayle. Gayle Benson has already been approved by the NBA as her husband\u2019s successor when it comes to making decisions concerning the franchise. Tom Benson had long planned to leave the Saints and Pelicans to Gayle Benson, whom he married in 2004. His plan survived a lawsuit filed by a daughter from his first marriage , Renee, and her two children in 2015, claiming Tom Benson did not have the mental capacity to decide to leave his franchises to Gayle Benson, his third wife. He had originally planned to leave the teams to his granddaughter, Rita, but changed his mind and his will. A judge ruled that year in Tom Benson\u2019s favor . A final legal battle within the family to determine the value of the shares of the Saints and Pelicans that Tom Benson wanted to remove from his family\u2019s trusts was settled in 2017 . So Gayle Benson was in place to assume her husband\u2019s role as owner of his sports teams when he died. \u201cAs we mourn the loss of Tom Benson,\u201d NBA spokesman Mike Bass said in a statement Friday, \u201cwe know the New Orleans Pelicans are on strong and stable footing as Gayle Benson, currently the team\u2019s Alternate Governor, assumes control of the franchise.\u201d Gayle Benson addressed the team soon after her husband\u2019s death last week, and \u201cbasically said that everything will remain the same,\u201d Pelicans coach Alvin Gentry said Sunday. \u201cEverything is stable and in place.\u201d This is a crucial season for the Pelicans, with All-Star Anthony Davis trying to carry the team across the finish line and to the playoffs for the first time since 2014 . His task was made harder when his fellow All-Star, center DeMarcus Cousins, went down for the season in January after tearing his Achilles\u2019 in a win against the Houston Rockets . Cousins is an unrestricted free agent after this season and could have \u2014 at least before the injury \u2014 signed a five-year max deal starting next season for around $175 million. With Davis already on a $145 million max deal and guard Jrue Holiday just starting his $126 million contract this year, the team is essentially hard-capped this season. The Pelicans have gone about as far as they can go in outlay. (Despite that, New Orleans had enough roster flexibility to acquire Nikola Mirotic before the trade deadline .) All that led to speculation that Gayle Benson may want to sell one or both of the franchises soon. The Pelicans are one of just three teams \u2013 the Denver Nuggets and Portland Trail Blazers are the others \u2014 with no concrete plans to launch a G League franchise in the near future. GM Dell Demps said in a radio interview earlier this month that the team has put existing discussions for the G League \u201con pause\u201d after it looked like the Pelicans were working toward a deal with Pensacola, Fla., to house a G League affiliate. But a source who worked directly with Tom Benson during his purchase of the Pelicans said over the weekend that there was no doubt that Gayle Benson would keep both teams for the foreseeable future. \u201cThere may be some changes later, but nothing imminent or early,\u201d the source said. And any notion of the Pelicans moving any time soon is tempered by the reality that the Pels, thanks to the league\u2019s revenue sharing plan, don\u2019t really have much financial incentive to sell. New Orleans was one of 14 teams that reportedly lost money in the 2016-17 season, according to a report by ESPN.com last September . But after receiving its cut of revenue-sharing dollars from the league\u2019s higher-revenue franchises, New Orleans was no longer in the red. (The team also got a boost in its local TV ratings last season. According to Forbes, the Pelicans had the fifth-biggest year-over-year increase in local television ratings in the league, 17 percent, drawing an average 1.44 rating on FS New Orleans.) That is important, for the Pelicans are often among the first teams thought of these days as a potential move target. Seattle is moving quickly toward securing an expansion NHL franchise and working with the newly formed Oak View Group to renovate Key Arena for both NHL and NBA teams. That means the hunt for potential existing franchises to move to the Emerald City could accelerate in intensity in the next few years, especially with NBA expansion unlikely any time soon.  The Pelicans\u2019 lease at Smoothie King Center runs through 2024, though. And if Gayle Benson isn\u2019t looking for a quick flip of the franchise, the likelihood of anything happening, at the very least, before then appears remote. And the NBA has gone to great lengths to ensure the stability and viability of a franchise in New Orleans since the then-Hornets moved there from Charlotte in 2002. The franchise\u2019s history with the city and state has been one of deal making that has helped the franchise remain economically viable as it fights for audience share locally \u2014 the Pelicans are currently 25th in the league in attendance, per ESPN.com , at 16,418 per game. The NFL\u2019s Saints, which Benson also owned, are the clear top dog in town for fans\u2019 affections, but they had a 35-year head start on the basketball team. The state spent an initial $10 million to renovate what was then known as New Orleans Arena as a condition of the move from Charlotte. After Hurricane Katrina struck in 2005 and badly damaged the arena, the NBA arranged for the team to play in Oklahoma City for two seasons, but never once considered leaving the franchise there. Former NBA Commissioner David Stern was adamant that the team not only return to New Orleans after repairs to the arena be made, but also worked to bring the 2008 All-Star Game there. (The 2017 All-Star Game was also awarded to New Orleans after the NBA took the game from Charlotte, where it was initially supposed to be played, in the wake of the North Carolina General Assembly\u2019s passage of the controversial HB2 \u201cBathroom Bill.\u201d ) Smoothie King Center is owned by the state of Louisiana and is operated by SMG, the arena management company. SMG also runs the Mercedes Benz Superdome that houses the Saints. They get a base fee from the Louisiana Stadium and Exposition District, the state entity that owns the arena, as well as additional fees from non-NBA and NFL events at Smoothie King and the Superdome. Then, famously (or, infamously, depending on the Laker fan with whom you speak), the NBA bought the team from former owners George Shinn and Gary Chouest in 2010 for $300 million, keeping it from being sold to out-of-state owners while the league sought local ownership. (It was during the league\u2019s ownership of the team that Stern, acting as the team\u2019s de facto final decision maker while the league owned the team, vetoed a proposed three-team trade that would have sent Chris Paul from the then-Hornets to the Lakers .) Benson bought the team from the league for $338 million in 2012, ensuring that it would remain in New Orleans for the foreseeable future. He may not have been the only prospective owner for the Pelicans, but another source directly involved in the sale then said that Benson was the perfect prospective owner \u2014 \u201cgiven the proximity of the Arena and the Dome and the proficiency of the business operations that were already functioning,\u201d the source said. \u201cAnd he was a good owner, willing to commit resources to the team and the building (including a great new practice facility).\u201d The team then got a $50 million subsidy from the state, and was allowed to continue receiving an annual $3.5 million tax break, in a deal with the state in 2012 that extended the Pelicans\u2019 lease with the arena through 2024. The Pelicans reportedly received $40 million in naming rights from Smoothie King in 2014 , further monetizing the building. And the building has gotten good marks in fan surveys, ranking in the top 10 among all NBA arenas in overall satisfaction with the game experience. Attendance this season at Smoothie King Arena is holding steady from last year, when the Pelicans were 24th in the league in average attendance. The hope in the Big Easy is that Davis can lead the Pelicans to at least two additional home games there in April. After routing Boston Sunday night, New Orleans is in sixth place in the Western Conference , tied record-wise with fifth-place Utah and seventh-place San Antonio at 40-30. Davis has been sensational in Cousins\u2019 absence: since the All-Star break, he is averaging 30.8 points per game on 51.1 percent shooting, 11.8 rebounds and an insane 3.6 blocks per game. Davis casts a long shadow over the New Orleans sports world and perhaps only Saints quarterback Drew Brees, who won a Super Bowl there in 2010 , has more cache. Yet Tom Benson was the boss of both. His absence creates a void in the city that will be filled, one way or another \u2014 by continued great feats by great players, or by silence if they\u2019re no longer in town. * * * Longtime NBA reporter, columnist and Naismith Memorial Basketball Hall of Famer David Aldridge is an analyst for TNT. Paul also joins Jason Kidd, Gary Payton and John Stockton as the only players with 8,000 assists and 2,000 steals. Paul entered this season with 1,912 career steals, which had him at 16th place all-time in that category . Before reaching the 2,000-steal mark, Paul passed Los Angeles Lakers legend Kobe Bryant (1,944 career steals), Dallas Mavericks legend Derek Harper (1,957) and Hall of Famer Allen Iverson (1,983). Paul trails former Atlanta Hawks All-Star guard Mookie Blaylock (2,075) for 11th place on the all-time list. Hall of Famer and Utah Jazz legend John Stockton is the all-time leader in steals with 3,265 .  Here\u2019s a look back at some other notable steal milestone dates in Paul\u2019s career: * 100th career steal : New Orleans\/Oklahoma City Hornets 100, Chicago Bulls 95 (Feb. 1, 2006) * 500th career steal : New Orleans Hornets 104, Los Angeles Lakers 107 (April 11, 2008) * 1,000th career steal \u2014 New Orleans Hornets 108, Indiana Pacers 96 (April 3, 2011) * 1,500th career steal \u2014 LA Clippers 104, Golden State Warriors 121 (Nov. 5, 2014) In 2011, Paul became the fourth fastest player in NBA to history to record 1,000 steals, doing it in 420 games. The only players who did it faster are Michael Ray Richardson (383), Michael Jordan (362) and Alvin Robertson (351). In 2008, Paul set an NBA record with a steal in his 106th straight regular-season game . That feat took place on Dec. 17, 2008, breaking Alvin Robertson\u2019s mark set between November 1985 and December 1986. Michael Jordan had the third-longest streak in that category, swiping the ball in 77 consecutive regular-season games from March of 1988 to March of 1989. Paul\u2019s streak reached 108 games before it ended on Dec. 25 in a loss to the Orlando Magic . In his career, Paul has led the NBA in total steals five times (2005-06, \u201907-08, \u201908-09, \u201910-11 and \u201911-12) and steals per game six times (\u201907-08, \u201908-09, \u201910-11, \u201911-12, \u201912-13 and \u201913-14). It goes against competition. To me, that\u2019s cheating the fans. You give 100 percent all the time. That\u2019s just my personal opinion.\u201d Sam Worthen is the coach of the Washington Generals. Not familiar with the Washington Generals? If you\u2019ve heard of the Harlem Globetrotters, there\u2019s a good chance you\u2019ve heard of the Generals. They are the Globetrotters\u2019 perennial sparring partners, sad-sack straight men to basketball\u2019s crown princes of comedy. Only Wile E. Coyote has a longer losing streak against a single opponent, the Road Runner, than the Generals. They have lost somewhere between 14,000 and 17,000 times to the barnstorming Globetrotters since the Washington team was established in 1952 as regular, reliable fall guys. Look up \u201chapless\u201d in the dictionary and you\u2019ll see a sketch of Louis (Red) Klotz, Generals founder and longtime player\/coach, and his band of plucky but perpetually humbled teammates. The Generals have done business under a variety of aliases through the years \u2014 the Boston Shamrocks, the New Jersey Reds, the New York Nationals, International Elite, Global Select and the World All-Stars all have been pseudonyms \u2014 while achieving a remarkable consistency of results. In other words, if any basketball team has thrived with a strategy of winning-by-losing \u2014 as some NBA teams attempt to do each spring \u2014 it\u2019s the Generals. They have done their duty thousands of times over, sending fans of the clever Trotters home happy time and time again. Allegedly, that formula is about to change. \u201cIn the past, the Generals got so used to losing,\u201d Worthen said. \u201cThey had a lack of talent and poor coaching. Now we\u2019re changing everything around. We have [TNT NBA studio analyst] Kenny Smith as the general manager and myself as coach. \u201cWe took three years off for planning and preparation for how to beat the Globetrotters. We did a lot of scouting, Kenny and I, watching all kinds of tapes, looking at the Globetrotters\u2019 strengths and weaknesses. And we recruited a bunch of guys with special talents who believe they can win.\u201d Worthen was on the telephone, but you could almost hear in the background the sabers he was rattling. Between some telltale chuckles. \u201cIn the past, the Generals had guys who didn\u2019t think they could win. They were just \u2018out there.\u2019 The fans will notice this, because when we put the product on the court, they will see the talent we have.\u201d It sounded like a preseason spiel, but the Generals have been putting the product on the court since December, when the Globetrotters\u2019 2018 \u201cAmazing Feats of Basketball World Tour\u201d began. The groundwork Smith and Worthen put in during Washington\u2019s hiatus \u2014 Smith, by the way, declined to be interviewed for this piece, perhaps unable to keep his tongue firmly in cheek \u2014 began last summer. That\u2019s when a Generals entry competed in The Basketball Tournament , the winner-take-all, $2 million event. Worthen\u2019s squad did well playing the game seriously, but still lost in the first round to the Matadors , a team featuring former players from Texas Tech. The Generals had former NBA player Sundiata Gaines on their side then \u2014 he logged 113 NBA games from 2009-12 with the Utah Jazz, Minnesota Timberwolves, Toronto Raptors and New Jersey Nets. Their stars these days include guards Trey Bardsley and Shaquille Burrell and forward Aquill Baynard. \u201cIn the past, you didn\u2019t see anything like these guys on the Generals,\u201d Worthen said. \u201cKenny and I always have our eyes open, looking for talent. \u201cWe\u2019re bigger, we\u2019re faster and we\u2019re better than ever. [The TBT] was our heads-up to the Globetrotters. They know that we\u2019re up and coming.\u201d As for Worthen, 60, he earned his chops at all levels of basketball over a lifetime spent in the game. A native of Brooklyn, N.Y., he starred at Franklin K. Lane High, spent two seasons at McLennan Community College in Waco, Texas, then played at Marquette from 1978-80. He was sporting a shaved head before Michael Jordan made it cool, dishing no-look passes worthy of Magic Johnson and averaging a triple-double at McLennan before Russell Westbrook was born. In fact, Worthen gained much of his hoops reputation on asphalt, playing for longtime NBA writer Peter Vecsey\u2019s squads in New York\u2019s renowned Rucker Tournament. Still, he and Marquette coach Hank Raymonds carved out a working relationship. They helped Marquette to a combined 40-16 record and two NCAA tournament berths. At the end of his senior season, he was drafted in the second round of the 1980 Draft by the Bulls. Worthen spent one season with Chicago and another with Utah , averging 3.5 points, 1.7 assists and 14.0 minutes in a total of 69 appearances. Then came playing, winning and later coaching in the United States Basketball League, with teams in Connecticut and Florida (where NBA legend World B. Free was a teammate briefly). Shifting into coaching full-time in Puerto Rico\u2019s professional Baloncesto Superior Nacional, Worthen worked for \u201cseven or eight teams in 10 seasons,\u201d he said. In between, Worthen returned each year to New York, where he worked on projects with the state and the city in youth basketball. He was an assistant to Jeff Capel Jr. for three seasons (2001-04) with the Fayetteville Patriots in the NBA G League and part of Jeff Ruland\u2019s staff at Iona College (2004-07). Then he hooked up with the Generals, even before their reset. Obviously, Worthen\u2019s coaching record has tilted toward the L\u2019s since accepting the role as the Globetrotters\u2019 regular punching bag. \u201cThe only column we look at is on the left-hand side,\u201d Worthen said. \u201cThen when we get that digit for the left-hand side, yeah, that\u2019s what we\u2019re looking at. \u201cThe last time we had to use some ink in that column was January 5, 1971. That was in Martin, Tennessee, when the Generals beat the Globetrotters, 100-99.\u201d It\u2019s true. Forty-seven years ago, the Globetrotters were playing without their dribbling magician Curly Neal and the Generals were unusually competent. The headliners clawed back from a 12-point deficit to lead by a point with second remaining. That\u2019s when Klotz threw up a set shot that banged in. After Globetrotters star Meadowlark Lemon failed several times, with a couple of clock resets, to hit a hook shot, the Generals officially snagged their rare victory . \u201cThat\u2019s what we\u2019re looking forward to again. It\u2019s coming,\u201d Worthen said. \u201cLike they say, they\u2019re the best team in the world. So once we beat them, we become the best team in the world. \u201cThe toughest thing is staying up all night, watching tapes, breaking down video to see what we can do.\u201d So Worthen feels that the Generals are getting close to beating their nemesis\/meal tickets? \u201cNo, I wouldn\u2019t say that necessarily,\u201d he said, laughing. \u201cBut our main, No. 1 goal is to come up with that victory. We\u2019ve been working really hard on it.\u201d Surely that would generate headlines, snapping a losing streak that\u2019s 47 years old now. Worthen just chuckled some more. There is no compelling reason to tinker with the script. The Globetrotters draw the crowds, provide the schtick and dazzle just enough with dunks, crafty passes and circus shots to maintain their court cred. The Generals are there to suggest resistance, but the defensive intensity typically is on par with some past NBA All-Star Games. The Generals don\u2019t lack for opportunities to win. There are three squads of them, synched up with three squads of Globetrotters that divide their tours along the East Coast, in western and southern states, and internationally. On Friday, for instance, \u201cthe Globetrotters\u201d were scheduled for games in Loveland, Colo., Reading, Pa., and Bordeaux, France. Combined, they play about 400 games per five-month season. Worthen and his wife, Florine, live in the Bronx and have adjusted to having few or no home games. Never mind that \u201cWashington\u201d tag \u2014 Worthen\u2019s life is spent largely on the road, without NBA-level charter flights or per-diem pocket money. The grueling schedule and breakneck travel suggests the Generals and the Globetrotters don\u2019t exactly have NBA commissioner Adam Silver in their corner on the modern issue of rest. They routinely have days when they play both a matinee and, scurrying to another city, an evening game. \u201cYes, doubleheaders,\u201d Worthen said. There would be a strike in the NBA if the league tried to schedule those, Worthen was told. \u201cHow \u2018bout that? Yeah,\u201d he said, laughing again. \u201cAnd I love every bit of it.\u201d * * * Steve Aschburner has written about the NBA since 1980. That, more than Russ\u2019 triple-double fetish, is the story here. The offseason All-Star additions of Paul George and Carmelo Anthony haven\u2019t turned OKC into a super team or made a bump in terms of the bottom line, at least not yet, and time is hardly on the Thunder\u2019s side. Remember, this team is on the clock. The playoffs are fast approaching, and so is George\u2019s free agent decision on his future this summer. There\u2019s no question that those two are intertwined. Unless there\u2019s another gear that Oklahoma City hasn\u2019t shown after 70 inconsistent games, Westbrook might be staring at yet another remaking of his team this summer. If you\u2019ve lost count, it\u2019s at four right now. There was the Westbrook-Kevin Durant-James Harden Thunder, young talent on the rise, who reached the NBA Finals but eventually self-destructed when it was time to pony up and OKC got spooked by the luxury tax and Harden wanted his own team. Imagine: Assuming Harden gets the vote in his favor this year, that\u2019s three MVPs once on the same roster. There was the Westbrook-Durant Thunder, where the dynamic deuces carried the club to a string of great seasons, finally undone by untimely injuries and a collapse after leading Golden State 3-1 in the West finals and Durant\u2019s desire to join \u2018em after he couldn\u2019t beat \u2018em. The Westbrook-Victor Oladipo Thunder became purely a Westbrook vanity project, where Westbrook was free to roam and wreak havoc while poor Oladipo was, for the most part, a curious spectator and eventual trade chip to get George from Indiana (don\u2019t cry for Oladipo; he\u2019s doing OK now). And now it\u2019s down to this, Westbrook-George-Melo, a maddening project that too often gets caught in a fog against inferior teams while occasionally flexing the firepower that makes you wonder if they can be better in the playoffs than the regular season. Ask Westbrook about where the Thunder stand at this checkpoint of the season, and he\u2019s suspiciously non-committal: \u201cMan, we\u2019re just taking it one game at a time and see how far we can go. We know who we are and what we have and what we\u2019re capable of doing.\u201d That\u2019s not what Harden would say about the Rockets or Durant about the Warriors, although to be fair, those teams seem beyond the reach of the rest of the league, not just Oklahoma City. It\u2019s perhaps wise to hedge a bit anyway with the Thunder going forward, because the clues they\u2019ve left behind are all so perplexing. The pluses are obvious, starting with Westbrook. His only flaw this season is 3-point shooting, where he\u2019s under 30 percent. Otherwise, the energy is still there, as evident by his relentless rebounding; he\u2019s the only player under 6-foot-5 who\u2019s in the top 40 . And he\u2019s still finding teammates despite what his usage rate might suggest. He\u2019s only taking three fewer shots this season despite the addition of players who were No. 1 options on their previous teams, and that could be a nit-pick. Blame that on Westbrook\u2019s eternal belief that he can beat his man off the dribble or hit the pull-up jumper. He\u2019s often a victim of his supreme confidence in himself. Even Anthony, who\u2019s most affected by Westbrook\u2019s 21 shots a night, is impressed. \u201cWhat he\u2019s able to do on nightly basis, what it takes to do that, the focus you have to have, for me to be a part of that, be alongside him, it\u2019s special,\u201d Anthony said. \u201cIt\u2019s something we all should appreciate what we have and who we have.\u201d It hasn\u2019t been an easy transition all around for Anthony. Leaving Manhattan for a sleepy Midwestern town that doesn\u2019t offer much after midnight has taken some adjusting, along with being away from his son, who still lives in New York. Yet it\u2019s the basketball side that finds Melo dealing with his mortality. When the season began, he chuckled at the thought of a reduced role or coming off the bench, and while he\u2019s still in the starting lineup, his role clearly has changed. It has hit him all at once: His surroundings, limitations and pecking order, and that\u2019s not easy for a star to swallow. This is the first time in memory that Melo hasn\u2019t been his team\u2019s lead singer, and this goes beyond 13 years in the NBA and his one year at Syracuse. Also, when he does have the ball, his freedom on the floor is different. Until now, Melo could do as he pleased, shoot from where he wanted. With OKC, in this system, he rarely posts up anymore, which means fewer free throw chances \u2014 he\u2019s taking just 2.6 per game , down from an average of seven during his Knicks days, although age (33) and surgery also plays a part. \u201cYou just have to accept what this team needs,\u201d he said. \u201cI was able to accept that, although it took a while. Now the game is just coming to me. It\u2019s a different style than what I\u2019ve been used to but when we\u2019re playing like this and (Russ) is doing what he does and everyone playing their role, we\u2019re successful.\u201d The ball was constantly in Melo\u2019s hands for his entire basketball life and every offense he\u2019s been involved in ran through him. He dictated everything. Now? He\u2019s a spot-up shooter who, for the most part, waits for his cue from Westbrook. Coach Billy Donovan said: \u201cWe\u2019ve had different talks through the year about what we need from him to help our team, and he\u2019s been mature enough to go through that. Carmelo has been the best in this league for a long time at what he does, but obviously not everybody\u2019s going to get everything they had in the past. \u201cPaul doesn\u2019t have the ball as much as he did, Carmelo\u2019s not going to score as much as he did, same with Russell,\u201d Donovan said. \u201cIt speaks to their character about wanting to win that they\u2019re willing to figure out what can we do to try and come up with a way to play that\u2019s the best version of ourselves. All those guys had to take less. Winning\u2019s important to all of them.\u201d Winning in the playoffs could ultimately push George in one direction or another once he hits free agency in July. That\u2019s a lot of pressure awaiting the franchise next month. OKC lost to the Rockets in five games last April, although the Thunder\u2019s first-round matchup this time should be more favorable if they get the 4 or 5 seed. George still maintains that his belief in OKC\u2019s ability to win a title will factor heavily in whether he stays or leaves. \u201cWe just have to pick up the pace and keep it rolling,\u201d he said. \u201cIt\u2019s all coming together for us, we just have to keep it going.\u201d The remaining schedule isn\u2019t cooperating. The Thunder have only one game, the season finale against the Grizzlies, against a team that\u2019s not fighting for a playoff spot or seeding. That means Donovan probably won\u2019t have the luxury of resting any of his most important players, and that includes Adams, who\u2019s dealing with a bum ankle. Also, they\u2019re without Andre Roberson, their next-best defender after George, the rest of the way. With a strong regular season finish, OKC could get third place, behind the Warriors and Rockets, right where they were projected to be. Or, in the event of a struggle, which is also possible given their behavior pattern, they could be seventh or eighth and see either team. \u201cOne game at a time,\u201d said Westbrook. If nothing else, OKC is destined to get the usual from Westbrook, who surprisingly isn\u2019t in the conversation for MVP, yet could average a triple or close enough. That surprises the people around him. \u201cHe\u2019s engaged,\u201d said Donovan. \u201cIt speaks to his control of the game and how hard he plays. It\u2019s a heck of a milestone, getting 100 triple doubles. He doesn\u2019t have to take a shot to impact the game.\u201d Yet if Westbrook doesn\u2019t win a playoff round or two, he could be staring at another off-season of changes around him. Anthony isn\u2019t going anywhere because the $27 million he\u2019s due in his option year is more than anyone will pay. George has other avenues to explore, if he chooses. If George leaves, then there\u2019s a very real chance Westbrook will be a star on his own island for some time. That\u2019s because it\u2019s tough to get free agents to come to OKC, which is why GM Sam Presti has worked overtime trying to trade for help (Oladipo, George, Melo, etc.). Put aside for the moment the perception, true or not, that few would want to play next to Westbrook. Oklahoma City, in spite of its charms, isn\u2019t the type of place that\u2019s on radar for stars. OKC got Durant, Westbrook and Harden because they were drafted, and Westbrook stayed for 205 million reasons . Would Melo in his prime sign in OKC? LeBron? Anybody who\u2019s an A-list star? That\u2019s an issue for this offseason. Right now, all the promise that Westbrook, George and Melo generated in the preseason needs to make an appearance this spring, if that\u2019s possible. After 70 games, how many more games does the Oklahoma City Thunder need in order to show us they\u2019re better than what the record says they are? Any rational observer of the league has paid attention to what he\u2019s done for the Houston Rockets this season has already concluded as much. Harden has created enough separation during the Rockets\u2019 magical run to distance himself from perhaps the deepest group of legitimate candidates in recent years. It\u2019s a group that includes four-time MVP winner LeBron James, two-time winner Stephen Curry, 2014 winner Kevin Durant and, perhaps most notably, reigning Kia MVP Russell Westbrook . The Oklahoma City Thunder superstar stormed to the MVP in 2016-17 on the strength of his historic season , becoming the first player since Hall of Famer Oscar Robertson to average a triple-double . Westbrook\u2019s position on the edge of the debate this season, however, serves as the most compelling argument for this MVP race being one of the deepest in years. He collected his 100th career triple-double Tuesday in a win against the Atlanta Hawks and is is averaging a near triple-double (25.3 points, 10.2 assists and 9.6 rebounds) for the second straight season . Yet Westbrook probably won\u2019t finish in the top five of the MVP race this season. \u201cThat\u2019s not a knock on Russ, it\u2019s a testament to the health of the league right now,\u201d an Eastern Conference executive said. \u201cIt\u2019s a nod to all of these guys who are having unbelievable seasons. Benson, who has also owned the NBA\u2019s New Orleans Pelicans since 2012, was 90. The NFL and NBA teams announced Benson\u2019s death on Thursday . He had been hospitalized since Feb. 16 with flu symptoms. When the NBA took over the financially troubled New Orleans Hornets in late 2010 and spent more than a year trying to find a permanent owner, Benson finally stepped in and bought the club for $338 million. The club is now known as the Pelicans and its estimated value exceeds $1 billion. NBA Commissioner Adam Silver said he\u2019d remember Benson as big-hearted and gracious, and praised the way he not only ran the Pelicans, but also hosted two All-Star weekends in New Orleans, the second after it had been moved from Charlotte following state legislation deemed discriminatory by the NBA. \u201cThe NBA family mourns the loss of New Orleans Pelicans owner Tom Benson,\u201d Silver said. \u201cBig-hearted and gracious, Tom topped off a distinguished business and sports career by acquiring the Pelicans in 2012. During his tenure, he hosted two highly-successful All-Star Games, rebranded the franchise and installed a first-class organization. He was a dear friend to me and so many others in the sports world, and the loss of his authentic and unique presence will leave an enormous void. We send our heartfelt condolences to Gayle, their family, the Pelicans and Saints, and his countless friends.\u201d Benson bought the Saints in 1985 when it appeared the club would be sold to out-of-state interests and perhaps moved out of Louisiana. He paid $70 million for the team, which is now worth close to $2 billion Benson\u2019s business acumen helped turn the Saints from a perennial also-ran into a contender \u2013 and the 2009 NFL champion. Yet his ownership also was less flatteringly marked by the 2012 bounty scandal and reports Benson did not want to bring the team back to New Orleans from San Antonio after Hurricane Katrina devastated the city in 2005. \u201cTom Benson\u2019s contributions to New Orleans and the National Football League were legendary. He purchased a team that had never had a winning season; by the third year of his ownership, the Saints were in the playoffs,\u201d NFL Commissioner Roger Goodell said. \u201cTom kept the Saints together through the tragedy of Hurricane Katrina, and his decision to bring the team back to New Orleans gave the entire region hope and confidence that they would recover.\u201d Benson also became a leading New Orleans philanthropist. He helped fund Tulane\u2019s on-campus football stadium and the cancer treatment center at Ochsner Medical Center. The home of the NFL\u2019s Hall of Fame game in Canton, Ohio, also was renamed for Benson after an $11 million gift, the largest in the hall\u2019s history. Benson \u201cpossessed a deep passion for the game and was committed to its growth,\u201d said Pro Football Hall of Fame President David Baker, who was also the Arena Football League commissioner when Benson owned an arena team called the VooDoo. Dallas Cowboys owner Jerry Jones called Benson a \u201cfriend and ally\u201d whose marketing and finance expertise guided the NFL\u2019s Finance Committee. \u201cHe was astute, exceptionally smart and an accomplished sportsman and businessman,\u201d Jones said. \u201cHe pumped life and enthusiasm into a community and a franchise that had no winning seasons before he became involved.\u201d Benson\u2019s death comes on the heels of an acrimonious family split that has caused some uncertainty about the future of his professional teams. He made it known in January 2015 that he wanted his third wife, Gayle, to inherit complete control of the Saints and Pelicans, and Greg Bensel, the senior vice president of communications for both clubs, said the NFL and NBA have both approved Benson\u2019s succession plan. However, Benson\u2019s disowned daughter, Renee, and her two children, Rita and Ryan LeBlanc \u2013 who had long been in line to take over the Benson business empire \u2013 have vowed to prove their patriarch was manipulated against them while in a mentally enfeebled state. They lost a 2015 legal struggle for control of the teams, but could still contest Benson\u2019s will. Fans, however, will remember Benson\u2019s heavy New Orleans accent and parasol dances along the sideline after victories. He cut quite the figure among NFL owners when he first joined the league. His jovial game-day persona turned hardheaded, however, when it came to business. In 2001, he negotiated an unprecedented $187 million in concessions and state subsidies to keep his team playing in the Superdome through 2010 \u2013 a deal Benson said was necessary to succeed in small-market New Orleans. That lease was followed by another unusual arrangement in which the state stopped paying direct subsidies to Benson, but committed to relocate numerous state offices in a Katrina-damaged high-rise next to the Superdome \u2013 at above-market rents \u2013 if Benson rehabilitated the building, which is now called Benson Tower. Until recent years, fans often questioned whether Benson\u2019s desire for profit outweighed his loyalty to his native city. With the Superdome and much of the city wrecked by Katrina, the Saints temporarily relocated to San Antonio, where Benson had many business holdings. When officials there said they were talking to Benson about what it would take to keep the team, Katrina-weary residents in New Orleans reacted angrily. Graffiti reading, \u201cWarning, Tom Benson inside,\u201d was scrawled on numerous foul-smelling, discarded refrigerators that lined sidewalks around the city. But when the Saints returned to a still-reeling New Orleans in early 2006 \u2013 or were forced to by the league, which would not allow Benson to abandon the Big Easy \u2013 the region\u2019s fans responded with more than a decade of consecutive sellouts. The Saints became a source of inspiration during the tough early days of post-Katrina rebuilding. They improved from 3-13 in 2005 to 10-6 under new coach Sean Payton, and then advanced to the franchise\u2019s first NFC championship game. A Super Bowl title came in February 2010. Scandal followed two years later when the NFL uncovered a bounty system in which Saints players earned improper, off-the-books cash bonuses for hits that hurt or sidelined opponents. Payton wound up serving an unprecedented full-season suspension in 2012 and the Saints were fined $500,000 \u2013 even though Benson had ordered general manager Mickey Loomis to shut down the bounty program when the owner was told of it. One of four children, Benson grew up in the hardscrabble Seventh Ward of New Orleans. In 1945, Benson served in the U.S. Navy aboard the USS South Dakota. Then he studied business and accounting at Loyola University in New Orleans, went to work as a bookkeeper for Cathey Chevrolet Co., and was sent to San Antonio as a 29-year-old in 1956 to manage a Chevrolet dealership as a junior partner. Six years later, he took full control of the company and established his own dealership. He built his fortune primarily in the automobile business, as well as in banking and real estate. More recently, he got involved in thoroughbred horse racing and took over Dixie Beer. But his passion later in life was the Saints. He kept an office at the training facility and was frequently seen there and at training camp in a golf cart watching practices. Tom Benson and his first wife, Shirley, had three children, two of whom preceded their father in death. He also had several stepchildren from his marriage to his second wife, Grace, who died in 2003. In addition to his wife, estranged daughter and her two children, Tom Benson is survived by grandchild Dawn Jones, the daughter of Tom Benson\u2019s late son, Robert."},{"id":47037,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/pelicans-owner-tom-benson-passes-away-90","date":"2018-03-15","title":"New Orleans Pelicans, Saints owner Tom Benson dies at age 90","content":" NEW ORLEANS \u2014 Tom Benson, 90, passed away peacefully Thursday, March 15, at Ochsner Medical Center with his wife Gayle Marie Benson at his side. Mr. Benson was hospitalized with the flu on Feb. 16. Thomas Milton Benson, Jr. was born on July 12, 1927 in New Orleans to Thomas Milton Benson Sr. and Carmelite Marie Pintado Benson. Raised in the 7th Ward neighborhood of New Orleans, Mr. Benson graduated from St. Aloysius High School (now Brother Martin) in 1944. Following his high school graduation, he enrolled at Loyola University New Orleans to study business and accounting. He interrupted his education to enlist in the U.S. Navy, where he was assigned to the USS South Dakota. Upon the conclusion of World War II, Mr. Benson returned home to New Orleans and continued his business administration studies. In 1948, he went to work as a bookkeeper for the Cathey Chevrolet Co. in New Orleans and by 1956, at age 29, Mr. Benson was on his way to manage a Chevrolet dealership as a junior partner. Six years later, he took full control of the company and established a multi-dealership organization with outlets throughout the New Orleans area and South Texas. In 1972, Mr. Benson entered the banking business and eventually took his banking network public as Benson Financial World. In 1985, Mr. Benson purchased the New Orleans Saints after learning that the NFL franchise was on the verge of being sold to parties interested in relocating the team. Mr. Benson purchased the Saints on May, 31, 1985. In 1987, the team posted its first winning season and advanced to the playoffs for the first time. Since being purchased by Mr. Benson in 1985, the Saints have earned 11 playoff berths, six division titles and captured Super Bowl XLIV in 2009, building a foundation for continued success, on and off the field. Known among his peers in the NFL as an important leader, Benson\u2019s input on the league level was instrumental, serving as chairman of the league Finance Committee for three separate stints. He also played a major role in New Orleans become a Super Bowl host five times since his purchase of the team. In 2012, Mr. Benson purchased the National Basketball Association\u2019s New Orleans Hornets franchise from the NBA, which the following season, he rebranded and had renamed the New Orleans Pelicans. Through his sports teams, business interests and the Gayle and Tom Benson Foundation, Mr. Benson was dedicated to assisting a myriad of charitable, faith-based and educational causes in the New Orleans and South Texas communities. Under Mr. Benson\u2019s direction his businesses and sports teams annually have put millions of dollars back into the community in financial support, in-kind donations, charitable appearances and the donations of goods and services. Over 50 deserving charitable organizations, foundations and agencies have annually received financial support from Mr. Benson. He doesn\u2019t even particularly like standing out there to shoot, since it comes at the expense of other, more active and creative maneuvers on the basketball court \u2014 as his 33rd birthday approaches \u2014 that he still enjoys. But if standing out there to shoot is what\u2019s required in Houston Rockets coach Mike D\u2019Antoni\u2019s system, then that\u2019s what Ariza is going to do.  Because he and his teammates are having too much fun this way. They put their stretch of 22 victories in the past 24 games on the line again Thursday against the LA Clippers. \u201cOne thing I know, these guys are hungry for something,\u201d Ariza said the other day, not just of the All-Hall (of Fame, in time) backcourt of James Harden and Chris Paul but of the squad overall. \u201cThey\u2019re hungry to win a championship. And there\u2019s really no ego there. We\u2019re able to communicate \u2014 I think communication is always the biggest thing allowing you to take that next step. And we\u2019re having fun. We don\u2019t feel pressure, we just go out and play.\u201d Ariza, on a team laden with veterans, is one of Houston\u2019s most reliable rudders. Fourteen seasons and 929 regular-season games into a career that got off to a shaky start \u2014 the No. 43 pick in the 2004 Draft , by a losing New York Knicks team \u2014 Ariza is a constant for the Rockets. With Harden and Clint Capela, Ariza is one of three players whose run with the franchise pre-dates D\u2019Antoni\u2019s arrival in 2015. But then, Ariza pre- or post-dates a lot of coaches\u2019 arrivals \u2014 he has played for 12 of them, from Lenny Wilkens and Larry Brown early in his career, through Stan Van Gundy and Rick Adelman, to Kevin McHale and D\u2019Antoni. Born in Miami, raised in Los Angeles, Ariza has changed teams six times. And changed his game considerably in the process.  How Ariza played when he reached the NBA compared to now reflects the evolution of the league\u2019s prevailing offensive strategies. In his first four seasons, divvied up between the Knicks, Magic and Lakers, Ariza used his quickness and his lean, 6-foot-8, 215-pound frame to slash to the basket or shoot from inside the opponent\u2019s defense. Only 3.8 percent of his field-goal attempts came from outside the arc; nearly one quarter of his shots were the \u201clong twos\u201d now considered so abhorrent. Ariza shot them at a poor rate (33.9 percent) but at least he was more accurate there than he was on 3-pointers (20.9). Compare that to Ariza\u2019s last four seasons, all in Houston (with the past three under D\u2019Antoni). Nearly two-thirds of his shots (64.4 percent) have been 3-pointers. His long twos are down to 5.5 percent, and 87 percent of his field-goal attempts come either from 3-point range or in the restricted area. The game has evolved and Ariza has adapted. \u201cHe\u2019s been about the same as he\u2019s always been,\u201d D\u2019Antoni said. \u201cWhich is good, I\u2019m definitely not complaining. He might be shooting it a little better this year. Maybe making a few more drives to the basket, getting a little more comfortable with what we do.\u201d Ariza has occasionally been putting the ball on the floor and attacking to counter the perimeter-focused resistance Houston faces. That anyone has even noticed, and considers it a new wrinkle, amuses Ariza, because he always had that as part of his game. \u201cWhen I first got into the NBA, that\u2019s what I was pretty good at, getting to the basket,\u201d he said. \u201cAs my career played on, I developed a jump shot. I\u2019m just trying to do what my teammates need me to do. The defense takes away certain things, so in order to be successful and help my team, I have to do other things.\u201d Like a lot of players new to D\u2019Antoni\u2019s style, Ariza had to dial up his willingness to shoot the ball. That\u2019s a mindset that often doesn\u2019t come naturally to guys who haven\u2019t traditionally been first or second scoring options. \u201cJust being aggressive is the main thing,\u201d Ariza said. \u201cOne hundred percent you\u2019ve got to be aggressive. When you\u2019re not aggressive, you won\u2019t help this team at all. It messes up the whole flow.\u201d Said D\u2019Antoni: \u201cIt takes a little bit \u2014 even Chris Paul is going through that process. To catch and shoot takes a pretty quick trigger.\u201d At 12.1 points and 9.7 field-goal attempts per game, Ariza is Houston\u2019s No. 5 scorer. As a 3-and-D guy, the latter is a little more important than the former. His offensive (113.3) and defensive (105) ratings are solid \u2014 and he flexed new skills as a back-seat singer in one of Paul\u2019s State Farm commercials \u2014 but Ariza\u2019s efficiency ratings have him pegged as a below-average NBA player the past four seasons. That\u2019s one area in which the analytics-devoted Rockets might quibble with the numbers. Ariza has started all 54 games this season and is averaging 34.6 minutes. \u201cTrevor\u2019s a blue-collar player who does everything,\u201d veteran big man Nene said. \u201cHe can defend, he can pass, he can shoot it well. He can do multiple things in a game, and of course the way he deals with the players is very important. He is open to anyone in here. Everybody likes him, he\u2019s a leader and he makes everybody better.\u201d Here\u2019s a for-instance: D\u2019Antoni credited the Rockets\u2019 veterans, Ariza among them, for making sure bad habits didn\u2019t creep in during the team\u2019s recent 17-game winning streak. Coaches often dread such streaks, because it\u2019s harder to bend players\u2019 ears without a few teachable-moment losses. Not these days, though. \u201cIf we\u2019re not setting the right picks or boxing out,\u201d D\u2019Antoni said, \u201cbefore they even get back to the bench, they\u2019ve already discussed it and got it.\u201d Said Ariza, who won a championship ring with the Lakers in 2009 and is hoping for a second this spring: \u201cWe know how to police ourselves. He lets us be creative. He lets us do our thing. And he\u2019s there for reinforcement, the staff\u2019s there for reinforcement. We appreciate it. We do our best not to let him down.
