The Warriors play host to the Los Angeles Lakers Wednesday night at Oracle Arena ( 10:30 p.m. ET, ESPN ). \u201cOur record was good before, too,\u201d Warriors coach Steve Kerr said Sunday in Minneapolis. \u201cThe change was basically to give us a little burst early \u2013 JaVale\u2019s a burst guy, he\u2019s a high energy player. We were getting off to very slow starts. We saw some things on tape, for how teams were guarding us, and we felt that having that lob threat made some sense to start.\u201d McGee, 30, in his 10th NBA season, has started 13 games in all, averaging 13.7 minutes, 6.4 points, 4.0 rebounds and 1.2 blocks while shooting 69.6 percent. But his impact on the Warriors overall since Kerr went to him for a transfusion is far greater. Prior to All-Star Weekend, Golden State essentially was breaking even early in its games, posting a 111.9 offensive rating and 111.3 defensive rating in 58 contests. Since McGee got swapped into Zaza Pachulia\u2019s spot, both those numbers have improved: 123.3 offensive rating, 103.2 defensive. \u201cWe play center by committee,\u201d Kerr said. \u201cZaza\u2019s going to play a role. Jordan Bell, David West, [Kevon] Looney, they\u2019ve all got to contribute for us. But right now we like JaVale starting and he\u2019s playing well. \u201cHe\u2019s gaining some confidence. He\u2019s had a tough season in the consistency of his minutes \u2013 it\u2019s harder to play, harder to perform at a high level when you don\u2019t know when you\u2019re going in. I don\u2019t know how many games I didn\u2019t even play him at all. But I\u2019m really proud of JaVale for how he hung in there and handled himself while he was out, working hard just like Zaza\u2019s doing now.\u201d McGee has kept a relatively low profile, taking over for Pachulia as the Warriors\u2019 lone non-All-Star starter. He quipped recently to Warriors\u2019 beat writer Connor Letourneau of the San Francisco Chronicle that his one big adjustment to this new role is simply drinking \u201cmy energy drink earlier.\u201d This is his longest unbroken string of starts since 2011-12 in Washington. Since then, through stints with Denver, Philadephia, Dallas and Golden State, McGee started only 26 times in his next 299 appearances. Yet he brings a game at both ends not unlike center DeAndre Jordan for the Clippers. \u201cHe\u2019s almost a defense unto himself just because of the shot-blocking and the length,\u201d Minnesota coach Tom Thibodeau said of McGee. \u201cPachulia is going to be a physical, body guy. McGee is length and athleticism. And then of course, if David West plays, it\u2019s veteran savvy.\u201d McGee had one of his poorer performances Sunday at Minnesota , scoring two points with three rebounds and finishing firmly in the red at minus-15. Pachulia, by contrast, had 16 points and 11 boards off the bench. But McGee still is a plus-28 in the nine games he has started since the break.  \u201cI think he doesn\u2019t get a lot of credit that he deserves,\u201d Wolves veteran forward Taj Gibson said. \u201cHe\u2019s changed his whole game around, changed his whole mentality around, being on a winning team. He does everything the right way. You haven\u2019t heard any kind of crazy things about him in the papers, so I\u2019m truly happy for him. \u201cThat\u2019s what happens when you play for a playoff-bound team, a team that is trying to do something special. It kind of make everybody better. He\u2019s just an athletic big. He\u2019s one of those guys who, no matter how many years he\u2019s been in the league, he\u2019s always a lob threat. He\u2019s always a weak-side shot blocker. I\u2019ve got to be on my toes to keep him off the boards.\u201d Said Kerr: \u201c[It is] important for our guys to understand \u2013 if they don\u2019t know me by now, I mean, I play everybody \u2013 at some point you\u2019re going to get your chance. So you have to stay ready. That\u2019s exactly what JaVale\u2019s done.\u201d Like any NBA player, McGee still is capable of an occasional bonehead moment. But he has the Warriors laughing with him rather than at him, and that makes all the difference. Steve Aschburner has written about the NBA since 1980. Victor Oladipo, Indiana Pacers. Across the board career-highs \u2014 points (23.6), rebounds (5.3), assists (4.3), steals ( a league-leading 2.2 ), field goal percentage (.472), 3-point percentage (.369) \u2014 and, he\u2019s shooting 80.4 percent from the line. Per NBA.com\/Stats, among all regular guard starters this season (60 or more games), Oladipo is second in the league in Defensive Rating (101.7, behind only Boston\u2019s Jaylen Brown). He looked like a high-volume, low production player his first four seasons in the league. He\u2019s changed that narrative with one outstanding season, and looks for all the world like a building block for Indy with Myles Turner going forward. 2. Jamal Murray, Denver Nuggets. He\u2019s embraced being the every day point guard for the Nuggets, seemingly with very little adjustments or problems, while raising his averages (16.2 points, 3.6 rebounds and 3.2 assists per game) and shooting the ball much better this season on twos and 3-pointers. 3. E\u2019Twaun Moore, New Orleans Pelicans. It\u2019s hard to judge with certainty whether he\u2019s just benefiting playing off of Anthony Davis (and, for half the season, DeMarcus Cousins), but Moore has been terrific in his first season as a full-time starter at the two, solidifying New Orleans\u2019 lineup. He\u2019s been outstanding behind the arc ( 41.6 percent on 3-pointers ) and pretty efficient shooting overall, while guarding his position \u2014 he\u2019s seventh among starting two guards this year in Defensive Rating . Honorable Mention: I didn\u2019t think you could give MIP to a guy with eight years in the league coming into the season, but Jrue Holiday has also been really, really good in New Orleans this season.  Steve Aschburner : Victor Oladipo is going to win this award and it won\u2019t even be close. Oladipo turned whatever pressure he felt from being traded by OKC and stepping into Paul George\u2019s sneakers in Indiana into extreme motivation and a laser focus on this season. The results have been astounding, with Oladipo playing his way to an All-Star selection while leading the Pacers to a 40-28 record that a lot of us figured would be more like 28-40 by now. For my second and third slots, I like a couple of big men : Houston\u2019s Clint Capela , who is the indispensable third side of that Rockets triangle ( they\u2019re 35-2 when Chris Paul, James Harden and Capela play together ). And the Lakers\u2019 Julius Randle , who since Jan. 1 is averaging 18.3 points on 57.4 percent shooting with 9.1 rebounds, compared to last season\u2019s 13.2 ppg, 8.6 rpg and 48.8 percent. Shaun Powell :  First: Victor Oladipo, Indiana Pacers. He went from riding shotgun in OKC to being the top gun in Indiana and made himself into a steady and reliable first option. Second: Spencer Dinwiddie, Brooklyn Nets. The Pistons gave up on him once . The Bulls gave up  on him twice . He began the season coming off the bench in Brooklyn. Then, everything changed once he was elevated to starter, averaging 13 points and 6.9 assists per game. Third: Clint Capela, Houston Rockets. His development from a raw and gangly center to a more polished finisher and rebounder has given the Rockets a much-needed inside guy on a team of shooters. John Schuhmann :  1. Victor Oladipo , who has been better (more efficient) in a much bigger role than he had with the Thunder. He has seen the league\u2019s second biggest increase in usage rate (among players that have played at least 1,000 minutes each of the last two seasons), has increased his scoring efficiency ( true shooting percentage ), and has seen just a minimal drop in assist-turnover ratio. In short, he\u2019s gone from sitting in the back seat for the Russell Westbrook Show to being the leading man for a team that has been just as good as his old one. 2. Domantas Sabonis. I prefer not to look at second-year players for this award, but Sabonis makes too compelling of a case. He\u2019s seen the league\u2019s eighth biggest increase in usage rate, the league\u2019s biggest increase in rebounding percentage, and the league\u2019s third biggest increase in true shooting percentage (among players who have taken at least 300 shots each of the last two seasons). He went from being an afterthought in the OKC offense to being a fulcrum on a team that\u2019s been better offensively than his old one . 3. Spencer Dinwiddie\u2019s poor shooting of late (he has the league\u2019s worst effective field goal percentage over the last five weeks ) has this spot on my ballot up for grabs. Other candidates include Ed Davis, Tyreke Evans, Brandon Ingram, Trey Lyles and Tomas Satoransky.  Sekou Smith :  Clint Capela is the first name I\u2019m putting on my list, which values organic improvement as opposed to say, a change in scenery that produces better results. The Rockets, as devastating as they\u2019ve been this season with Chris Paul in the fold, have gotten a huge lift from Capela\u2019s breakout season. There\u2019s a reason the Rockets got out of the Dwight Howard business a couple years ago. They clearly saw something in Capela and it\u2019s paying huge dividends this season. I know some people have probably forgotten about it already, but before he went down for the season with that torn ACL, Kristaps Porzingis was showing signs of being a potential two-way superstar . Who knows how he bounces back after a 10-month, or more, recovery? But he\u2019s second on my list. And finally, while he doesn\u2019t have the raw numbers on his side, it\u2019s hard to ignore the boost the Boston Celtics get when Terry Rozier is in the mix . Shaun Powell :  The Pacers win this contest and it\u2019s not even close. Remember, they were universally targeted for last place after the Paul George trade , which was roundly criticized. Or is this team lottery bound, regardless of Leonard\u2019s status?  * * * David Aldridge :  It looks bleak, to be sure.  According to Tankathon , the Spurs have the second-toughest schedule remaining in the league. But when you have the tradition of San Antonio, you don\u2019t fold, even when you don\u2019t have a team good enough to win a title, as certainly seems the case this season. The Spurs play it out, no matter what. It\u2019s one of the things I most admire about the franchise; like the Utah Jazz teams of the Jerry Sloan era , it can accept losing in the playoffs if the team has done all it could do in a given year. So the Spurs would, and should, play Leonard down the stretch. He is the team\u2019s best player and his presence will help Dejounte Murray immensely, if nothing else. And, unfortunately, you never know what can happen; every team, including the Houston Rockets and Golden State Warriors, is one ACL away from disaster. Steve Aschburner : Certainly they should bring Kawhi back if he\u2019s deemed to be healthy. This is a franchise that has played for the postseason for a long time, and that\u2019s still intact, given how closely the lower seeds are grouped in the West. Is there really that much difference between coach Gregg Popovich giving players occasional nights off vs. Leonard getting a bunch of weeks and, OK, months off to rest, rehab and recuperate? Who knows, this might start a new trend of star players being kept in bubble wrap until April, with the first 82 games devalued to exhibition level. Shaun Powell :  If you\u2019re suggesting the Spurs tank, let history show that the franchise was accused of doing so twice, and came away with David Robinson and Tim Duncan. We all know what happened after that. The foundation of the respected franchise is built on getting those Hall of Famers. That said, it would go against everything the Spurs stand for today to sit a player, much less their star, for the sake of the lottery. If he\u2019s healthy, he should play. And they\u2019d make the playoffs if he did. John Schuhmann :  First of all, I would ignore the standings in regard to the decision about Leonard.  If he\u2019s healthy and isn\u2019t risking further damage by playing, then he should play, because the Spurs certainly aren\u2019t going to be mathematically eliminated any time soon. And if he gets the green light, the Spurs should hit the gas. They play 12 of their final 14 games against teams with winning records (and another against the spoiler Los Angeles Lakers), but they can beat good teams when healthy and a 21st straight trip to the playoffs is worth playing for. Right now, they\u2019re tied for eighth place with the Jazz , who they will play in San Antonio next week . So they control their own destiny and folding would be silly.  Sekou Smith :  I don\u2019t believe in using the \u201cT\u201d word, not even hypothetically, after seeing that fine the league handed down to Dallas Mavericks owner Mark Cuban . He should definitely play if he\u2019s healthy enough to do so. Then we can let things play out in whatever way basketball\u2019s higher powers decide. That said, if there is one organization I\u2019d ride with at lottery time it\u2019s the Spurs. Sure, they are rarely in that space. To put that into perspective, starting point guard Dejounte Murray was not yet a year old when that disastrous season ended, while the man he replaced, Tony Parker, was a precocious teenager just finishing his first year at France\u2019s prestigious National Institute of Sport. But as has been well-chronicled over the years, it came with the silver lining of all silver linings: An unexpected victory in the ensuing Draft lottery, and the right to draft Tim Duncan . The rest is, quite literally, history: A run of consistent success never before seen in the NBA featuring five championships , 20 straight playoff appearances and 18 straight campaigns with at least 50 victories . (That run would stand at 20 as well were it not for the strike-shortened season in 1998-99, when the Spurs finished 37-13 \u2014 a 61-win pace over 82 games \u2014 en route to their first title .) Reports of the Spurs\u2019 imminent demise have been plentiful over the years, and invariably wrong. When \u201999 mainstays like David Robinson, Avery Johnson and Sean Elliott succumbed to age, they promptly reloaded with Parker and Manu Ginobili. And as that crew began to wear down, they made a shrewd draft-day trade for Kawhi Leonard and squeezed out another title in 2015 . When Duncan finally retired after 19 illustrious seasons in 2016? The Spurs won 61 games without him, and were well on their way to victory over the Warriors in Game 1 of the Western Conference finals before Leonard went down with a series-ending sprained ankle. But now, with only 15 games remaining in the 2017-18 campaign, even more streaks are in jeopardy. At 37-30, with 14 losses in their past 21 games, the Spurs must finish 13-2 to keep their string of 50-win seasons alive. With Leonard reportedly set to return from his lengthy battle with right quadriceps tendinopathy as early as Thursday, it\u2019s certainly possible. And 10 of those remaining games are at home, where the Spurs are a formidable 23-8. But those are about the lone positives. Otherwise, 13 of those games coming against winning teams, including one apiece with Western Conference leaders the Houston Rockets and Golden State Warriors. The Spurs\u2019 situation is even more dire given that they have zero margin for error, sitting in 10th place in the West and just one game away from missing the playoffs entirely, something an entire generation of NBA fans has never experienced. But with one streak snapped and several more on life support, could the long-prophesied end of the Spurs\u2019 historic dynasty be here? If you have an issue with the rankings, or have a question or comment for John Schuhmann, send him an e-mail or contact him via Twitter . * * *"},{"id":47864,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/morning-tip-nba-nbpa-addressing-mental-wellness-issues","date":"2018-03-12","title":"NBA, NBPA taking steps to further address mental wellness issues for players","content":" More than five years later, Keyon Dooling is still dealing with the fallout of the day that triggered him. \u201cThere\u2019s still a negative perception of my breakdown,\u201d Dooling said over the weekend from California, while he was watching his son play baseball. \u201cI have to prove to people that I\u2019m healthy and strong. I\u2019m not defined by that moment, but it was a defining moment in my life.\u201d Dooling, then, knows what could be in the offing for Kevin Love and DeMar DeRozan \u2014 two All-Stars \u2014 who became the latest NBA players to detail their public battles with mental wellness . Love wrote a first-person account last week in The Players\u2019 Tribune of the panic attack he suffered earlier this season. DeRozan spoke last month of the depression he\u2019s dealing with during what may be his most successful NBA season . Their disclosures came as the NBA and the National Basketball Players Association are close to naming a Director of Mental Health and Wellness, who will run an independent mental wellness program that is being jointly funded by the league and union. The league has had programs dealing with wellness be part of the Rookie Transition Program, Team Awareness meetings with players during the season and its Player Assistance Program over the years. Players who\u2019ve dealt with mental illness have provided testimony about dealing with the stresses of life as pro basketball player \u2014 the constant demands for money, attention, time and the like \u2014 in healthy ways. But the new office will be the most comprehensive program dealing with mental wellness created to date.  The mental wellness program \u2014 the product of almost a year of discussions between the league and union that began as the sides were working out the new Collective Bargaining Agreement \u2014 will allow players to seek treatment and counseling outside of the framework of their individual teams, if they want. Existing team physicians and other resources will still be available to them, too. The new director will have authority and a significant role for players who seek his help. But it is not clear if the director will have the ability to unilaterally decide if a player dealing with a mental wellness issue should not play in a given game or games to deal with those issues, regardless of what the player\u2019s team medical staff may think. This was a main point of contention, you may recall, between the Houston Rockets and forward Royce White, whom Houston selected in the first round of the 2012 Draft . White wanted his own personal doctors, all of whom were experts in mental wellness, or third-party doctors who could act as a go-between, to have a say in determining an overall wellness program for him, which could have included consulting on decisions such as when his Generalized Anxiety Disorder was at a point that he would not be able to play on a given night. He says the Rockets agreed to help formulate a protocol that laid out a treatment plan for him going forward, while White played in the G League. But White was traded to the Philadelphia 76ers in the summer of 2013 , having never played a game for Houston, before any formalized protocol was finalized. Other than a three-game stint with the Sacramento Kings the following season and a Summer League stint with the Clippers, White has not yet played in the NBA. The new mental wellness program also dovetails with the league reviewing mental health and wellness policies and programs in the NBA, WNBA and G League. Late last year, the NBA hired the Jed Foundation to serve as an advisor on mental wellness issues. The Foundation, founded by Donna and Phil Satow in 1998 after their son, Jed, committed suicide, seeks to prevent suicides in teenagers and young adults. Its Chief Medical Officer, Dr. Victor Schwartz, has been reviewing the NBA\u2019s policies. Schwartz has found strengths and gaps within individual teams, and has been working to fill some of the gaps at the team level. The NBA has been drilling deeper into youth basketball as well, engaging programs like Jr. NBA and NBA Fit with wellness messages and programs, realizing that physical fitness is not all of total health. Mental wellness outreach will be a part of the inaugural Jr. NBA World Championships, a global basketball tournament for players 14 and under, that will be in Orlando next August. Dooling is also part of the league\u2019s new initiatives. In January, he started working as a Player Wellness Counselor, going around the league to talk with NBA teams and players about trying to deal with problems before they metastasize the way they did in Dooling. Dooling was sexually abused as a child , first by a friend of his older brother, then by women in the Florida neighborhood in which he grew up. Dooling buried the events in his subconscious, going on to star at Missouri and was picked 10th overall in the 2000 NBA Draft by the Orlando Magic . He played 13 NBA seasons and became known as a good teammate, close with guys like Rajon Rondo when they were on the Boston Celtics together.  But Dooling was triggered when a man grabbed him in a restroom in Seattle in 2012 , just after he\u2019d agreed to re-sign with Boston. The event brought everything boiling to the surface. He began acting strangely and lost weight. His wife, Natosha, couldn\u2019t reach him emotionally; she called Dooling\u2019s mother trying to find out what was wrong, but no one knew \u2014 Dooling had never told Natosha about the abuse. Finally, in August, 2012, Dooling\u2019s behavior concerned his neighbors outside of Boston, where he and the family had returned to get ready for the season. He was outside with his children when a neighbor thought he was playing too roughly with his kids and called the police. Police arrived at the house and didn\u2019t arrest him, instead taking him to a mental health facility, where he signed in voluntarily and spent a week there as doctors found levels of medication that allowed Dooling to process clearly as he began dealing with his breakdown. It is a difficult thing for a professional athlete to be able to do, especially in real time, Dooling says. \u201cFrom my experience as a player, in order to make it to the apex in sports, you have to be almost flawless and perfect at every intersection,\u201d he said. \u201cWe\u2019re trained to just shut up and play. We\u2019re trained to not process and talk about our emotions, how we feel. The environment we come from. We don\u2019t process things. We internalize a lot. Our body acts out on us. It comes out in the form of anxiety, PTSD. A lot of people will self-care for the symptoms but they won\u2019t deal with the core issues. You have to do therapy. You have to make sure you have a great support system. There are a lot of variables you have to have to have total wellness.\u201d Dooling will report to the new Director of Mental Health and Wellness, serving as liaison between players and the program resources. \u201cI can respond and I\u2019m still pretty relevant,\u201d Dooling said. \u201cI played against most of these guys. They see a safety net in me. I\u2019ll be providing them with support and resources. We\u2019ll be able to respond in real time, not only doing preventative stuff, but infrastructure that will outlive all of us \u2026 in 20 years, this program will be further advanced than it is now. It will be able to help not only ballplayers but society in general. If we start taking it seriously, society will follow that. We have the capacity to scale our model. The most important thing is to get that director in place so we can grow organically.\u201d So many players in recent years have acknowledged they were dealing with mental health issues while playing: Metta World Peace , Delonte West , the late Eddie Griffin , Larry Sanders , White, former WNBA star Chamique Holdsclaw and Hall of Famer Jerry West . And now, in the last few weeks, so have DeRozan and Love, along with the Washington Wizards\u2019 Kelly Oubre, who told NBCSportsWashington.com last week that he also has dealt with anxiety . In The Players\u2019 Tribune story, Love detailed a panic attack he suffered Nov. 5 in a game against Atlanta . He said he was already \u201cstressed\u201d with family problems. After not feeling right throughout the first half, Love said everything \u201chit the fan\u201d early in the third quarter. \u201cCoach (Tyronn) Lue called a timeout in the third quarter,\u201d Love wrote. \u201cWhen I got to the bench, I felt my heart racing faster than usual. Then I was having trouble catching my breath. It\u2019s hard to describe, but everything was spinning, like my brain was trying to climb out of my head. The air felt thick and heavy. My mouth was like chalk. I remember our assistant coach yelling something about a defensive set. I nodded, but I didn\u2019t hear much of what he said. By that point, I was freaking out. When I got up to walk out of the huddle, I knew I couldn\u2019t reenter the game \u2014 like, literally couldn\u2019t do it physically.\u201d Love said Lue came up to him during the timeout, but he ran past him into the Cavs\u2019 locker room. \u201cI was running from room to room, like I was looking for something I couldn\u2019t find,\u201d Love wrote. \u201cReally I was just hoping my heart would stop racing. It was like my body was trying to say to me, You\u2019re about to die . I ended up on the floor in the training room, lying on my back, trying to get enough air to breathe.\u201d This came after DeRozan acknowledged that a tweet he sent out the morning of the All-Star Game \u2014 \u201cthis depression get the best of me\u201d \u2014 was far more than his quoting Kevin Gates\u2019 song \u201cTomorrow.\u201d DeRozan\u2019s battles with depression, a condition that was exacerbated in recent months by the illness of his father, Frank, who\u2019s been in an L.A. hospital with serious medical issues. \u201cHonestly, the response, I can honestly say that I wouldn\u2019t have even thought how the response, how it came out, I wouldn\u2019t have thought I\u2019d ever gotten anything like that,\u201d DeRozan told me recently . \u201cEspecially me. I\u2019ve never been one who wanted any type of attention, good nor bad. The response I got from people was so uplifting, positive, refreshing. It\u2019s crazy. It\u2019s crazy. But it made me feel good. You just look at certain things. People say \u2018you helped me. Because if you\u2019re going through something like this, I can get through it.\u2019 It\u2019s incredible. By far one of the most incredible things in my career that I\u2019ve witnessed outside of basketball.\u201d The National Institute of Health\u2019s National Institute of Mental Health estimates that, in 2016, more than 44 million Americans suffered from some form of mental illness, ranging from mild to moderate to severe, and impacting more than 18 percent of all U.S. adults. Of that 44.7 million, all with what NIMH calls AMI \u2014 Any Mental Illness, defined as a mental, behavioral or emotional disorder \u2014 10.4 million had a Serious Mental Illness (SMI), defined as \u201ca mental, behavioral, or emotional disorder resulting in serious functional impairment, which substantially interferes with or limits one or more major life activities.\u201d Title I of the Americans With Disabilities Act, passed in 1990 and which bars discrimination against individuals with mental health issues, requires an employer to provide what the Act calls \u201cReasonable Accomodation\u201d to qualified individuals with disabilities, \u201cunless to do so would cause undue hardship.\u201d Reasonable Accomodation, according to the ADA, should allow the employee to \u201cperform the essential functions of the position.\u201d ADA cites as an example a cleaning company that rotates its staff to different floors of a building on a monthly basis. \u201cOne crew member has a psychiatric disability,\u201d the ADA example continues. \u201cWhile his mental illness does not affect his ability to perform the various cleaning functions, it does make it difficult to adjust to alterations in his daily routine. The employee has had significant difficulty adjusting to the monthly changes in floor assignments. He asks for a reasonable accommodation and proposes three options: staying on one floor permanently, staying on one floor for two months and then rotating, or allowing a transition period to adjust to a change in floor assignments. These accommodations are reasonable because they appear to be feasible solutions to this employee\u2019s problems dealing with changes to his routine. They also appear to be effective because they would enable him to perform his cleaning duties.\u201d Whether Reasonable Accommodation will be a part of the new NBA\/NBPA program is also uncertain. For years, the NBA left decisions about players\u2019 fitness to play in games while dealing with mental wellness issues to the individual team\u2019s physicians, many of whom were not experts in mental health. The Dallas Mavericks were the first team, in 2000, to employ a full-time psychologist, Dr. Don Kalkstein , as the Director of Sport Psychology\/Mental Skills. But now, many teams do. The Indiana Pacers, for example, hired Dr. Chris Carr as their Team Performance Psychologist in 2011 . He has an office at the team\u2019s practice facility, and frequently travels with the team on the road. \u201cI think he\u2019s a tremendous resource for all our guys,\u201d Pacers GM Kevin Pritchard said Sunday. \u201cAt some level, everybody uses him for a sounding board, some deeper than others. We give our players full access. We talked about it early, and our players feel like it\u2019s important, too. Not only do we give them the resource, but they have to use it.\u201d  Pritchard says the team is not privy to the details of conversations Carr has with team employees \u2014 which are not limited to the players. \u201cWe all want to have a stake in our players\u2019 health and wellness. We don\u2019t look at them as just players; we look at them as human beings. We want to help not only our players, but our coaches, everybody. Dr. Carr is not just with the players. He helps us all communicate better.\u201d But the issue of who decides the best course of action for a player \u2014 program director, or team, or some combination of both in conjunction with the player \u2014 is still a salient one. Teams don\u2019t even know, by way of contrast, if one of their players is in the league\u2019s Drugs of Abuse program, until after the player fails multiple drug tests or doesn\u2019t comply with a mandated treatment program. The medical director of the program is the only person with that information; once it\u2019s determined a player isn\u2019t in compliance, he can be suspended or, ultimately, terminated from the league. But there is no arguing with the medical director, unless the player can provide \u201cclear and convincing\u201d evidence , per cap expert Larry Coon\u2019s invaluable FAQ , to the league\u2019s grievance arbitrator that he was not at fault or negligent for the presence of a drug of abuse or sports enhancing drug. (The grievance arbitrator has the authority to reduce or rescind the player\u2019s penalty.) The player could argue, for example, that he didn\u2019t know or suspect he was taking a banned substance. What is clear is that the new program will be run independently of the teams, league and union. \u201cWe don\u2019t want players to be discouraged from getting help when they need it because they\u2019re concerned that it will get back to the team, or it may affect their play, or it may affect their next contract,\u201d NBPA Executive Director Michele Roberts told SB Nation\u2019s Paul Flannery in an interview last month . Yet even that can be debated when it comes to wellness. White is one of 6.8 million adults (3.1 percent of the U.S. population), according to the Anxiety and Depression Association of America, who lives with GAD. People with GAD, according to the ADAA , \u201cmay anticipate disaster and may be overly concerned about money, health, family, work, or other issues. Individuals with GAD find it difficult to control their worry. They may worry more than seems warranted about actual events or may expect the worst even when there is no apparent reason for concern.\u201d But White believes, strongly, that transparency when it comes to mental wellness is crucial. Even something as seemingly innocent as anonymity for players who use the new wellness program, White argues, helps contribute to the continued stigma about mental wellness. No one, for example, would seek anonymity if they had a sprained ankle or separated shoulder. Why, then, is this treated differently, he asks?  \u201cThe articulated aspiration for a union that would call for a new policy, kind of in its genesis, should set the bar really high,\u201d White, now playing his second season for the London Lightning of the National Basketball League of Canada, said by phone Saturday. \u201cThe height of the bar shouldn\u2019t be that players need anonymity when it comes to mental health issues, which is what (Roberts) suggested, that everything\u2019s going to be kept private, with the independent people,\u201d White said. \u201cThe articulated aspiration should be a collaborative and comprehensive program that involves all of the parties involved, that is going to be founded and based on a newly introduced knowledge of what mental health is. And that\u2019s going to take the owners being educated; it\u2019s going to take the GMs being educated, and the coaches and the trainers and the ballboys and the players and everybody else, up and down the line. When that happens, the players won\u2019t need anonymity.\u201d White argues that Love\u2019s disclosure exemplifies the problem of granting or desiring anonymity. \u201cThink of what Kevin Love said about his panic attack, and think of the pressure that it added that he couldn\u2019t tell his teammates,\u201d White said. \u201cThink of the consequences of him not being able to tell his teammates. Those same consequences, you could map onto management as well, and the ownership. Ideally, and what we are seeing and continue to see, is the sort of optimal support and treatment plan for people with these conditions is that people with these conditions be afforded the opportunity to come forth with their struggles openly, and communicate them on an ongoing basis. \u201cThe reason why anonymity is kind of the default is because people have conceded that when certain decision-makers get a hold of certain information, they will do corrupt things with it. That shouldn\u2019t be the system. We have to be able to get over the hurdle of things like that. The reason they created HIPAA (the Health Insurance Portability and Accountability Act, which keeps medical information confidential) is \u2026 why? Because people don\u2019t want other people to have their medical information, because they\u2019ll do unjust things with that information \u2026 \u201cI know the NBA is a big corporation, but when it comes to decision-making, there\u2019s not that many parties involved. How many owners are there? There\u2019s an ownership group; it can\u2019t be that many. It\u2019s a situation where you could give a baseline education to where anonymity is maybe no longer needed. Maybe a player still does want to keep the anonymity \u2026 well, then, that\u2019s up to the player. But don\u2019t set the standard so that the players who would be up front, like me, are actually then persecuted for being up front.\u201d White also notes that the new program lays out many ideas he has been advocating since 2012 , including bringing in outside experts like the Jed Foundation who can help fill in some of the gaps in the league\u2019s knowledge and protocols. White has had no issues in Canada. \u201cOne of the misconceptions is that I was really asking for much in the first place,\u201d he said. \u201cThere\u2019s no special formula or plan that I really need. All I\u2019m saying is that should be an issue, is there a system that we can properly deal with it? \u2026 Here in Canada, the traveling isn\u2019t the same, so there wasn\u2019t a huge conversation about that. And as far as the mental health side goes, the owner straight up told me, \u2018you have anxiety. I know a lot of people who have anxiety. If you ever need any help or any support when it comes to anxiety, don\u2019t be afraid to come to me and we\u2019ll figure things out.\u2019 That\u2019s it. That\u2019s the potential of what support looks like.\u201d Dooling often tells the story of when his father died in 2009, and how, in the course of burying him, people he knew came up to him and asked for money (\u201calmost $200,000,\u201d he says), asked for pictures, and autographs, and jobs. He felt like he never really got a chance to mourn his loss. How does someone process that in a healthy manner? \u201cA lot of things are sexy on the surface, like money and fame,\u201d Dooling said. \u201cBut people try to run scams on us. Family members might try to take advantage of us. We\u2019re often thrust into the patriarch position and we might be the youngest member of the family \u2026 when it was time to pay the bill, all the family members look the other way. \u201cYou internalize those things. And it can have an impact on your body \u2026 but we\u2019re told, don\u2019t talk about it. Shut up. Suck it up. You\u2019re a man. And we develop this condition of not being able to express our emotions. Because I chose to go to therapy there have been a lot of members of my family who\u2019ve gone to therapy as well. It forced them to look at their own issues. My kids, my wife, my mother, my siblings, they\u2019ve started to do their work.\u201d * * * Longtime NBA reporter, columnist and Naismith Memorial Basketball Hall of Famer David Aldridge is an analyst for TNT. Most everyone figured there\u2019s be some kind of valedictory, a final lap when Wade was ready to retire, a ceremonial last contract with the team that took him with the fifth pick of the historic 2003 Draft , and for the player who became the most important player in the franchise\u2019s history (with all due respect to Alonzo Mourning, Glen Rice, Shaquille O\u2019Neal and LeBron James). But that was supposed to happen down the road, not when Wade still had some tread left on his tires. It didn\u2019t work for him in Cleveland and few thought it really would, with him starting, and then coming off the bench (though he actually wasn\u2019t that bad defensively). But when Cleveland\u2019s General Manager Koby Altman blew up his underachieving team, he did Wade the solid of sending him home for nothing, really, something that went over well in the Wade household . Yet Wade hasn\u2019t been back just for the fun and games. Life has smashed all South Florida in the face, with the horrific killing of 17 students at Marjory Stoneman Douglas High School on Feb. 14 \u2014 an awful Valentine\u2019s Day. One of the kids killed, 17-year-old Joaquin Oliver, had just become a U.S. citizen, and he was a huge Wade fan. His parents buried their son in Wade\u2019s jersey . It tied Wade up in knots emotionally, as you\u2019d expect; he dedicated the rest of the season to Joaquin and has done more than send thoughts and prayers. He met Joaquin\u2019s parents (as did his mother) and gave them a pair of custom-made shoes; he paid a surprise visit to the school last Friday ; he had an exhibit honoring the slain students set up this past weekend at Miami\u2019s Art Walk in the Wynwood neighborhood . He and his wife donated $200,000 toward defraying the expenses of local teenagers who are planning to go to the march in Washington, D.C. and other cities in two weeks supporting gun control reform, got a commitment from the Oklahoma City Thunder\u2019s Carmelo Anthony and challenged other NBA stars to pony up. In the midst of all that, he\u2019s not just been a show pony for the Heat. He\u2019s not the Finals MVP from 2006 anymore, but he can do so many things that were familiar to the Heat during their glory days. Not much homework was required \u2014 \u201cwe know him as well as anybody,\u201d Coach Erik Spoelstra said last week. \u201cHe\u2019s a family member, so we know how he is. It\u2019s also not like we didn\u2019t keep in touch.\u201d Wade\u2019s already helped win a couple of games off the bench \u2014 27 points in 25 minutes against the 76ers ; 16 points and six rebounds a few days later to help throttle Philly again. It has followed that the often-somnolent home crowds in Miami have reawakened, seeing not an apparition but a still-capable veteran, grateful to be home but aware that more is required of him these days than leading the Heat on a postseason run. Me: What are those moments like for you now, when the crowd is getting up and cheering even before you take off the warmups? Dwyane Wade: It\u2019s special, you know? It\u2019s something (different) about me when I have a Heat jersey on than when I had the Bulls or Cleveland jersey on. I\u2019ve been to all these cities. I\u2019ve been on the road. It\u2019s just something about Dwyane Wade with Miami on his chest. Our fans are amazing. It\u2019s definitely, a night like tonight when you\u2019re on a back to back and you\u2019re coming in late. But when you hear the crowd behind you and they\u2019re cheering, you get up, it gives you that extra motivation and energy to give them everything you have. \u2018Cause they came to see you. Might be their first time, you don\u2019t know. Me: I think we all figured you\u2019d wind up back here at some point, but did you think it would be this soon? DW: Nah. I mean, when I made the decision to go to Cleveland , I didn\u2019t think it would be this season. Definitely, I knew, I always wanted it to be one day. I think it was just perfect timing, too. It was just right, the timing to come back. Everything works out in its weird ways. I definitely think the timing was better than if I had come back at the beginning of the year. For the city, the state and the team, I felt like my leadership, my voice, my face was needed at that time I came back. Me: But in coming back, to that team as opposed to any other, how did you reconcile the fact that you can\u2019t be that guy you were in 2009, 2010? I can\u2019t be the Finals MVP. I have to be the best version of who I am now. DW: I\u2019m a realist. I\u2019m real to myself. I know who I am. I\u2019ve always been that way. I look in the mirror every day at myself, and if I can\u2019t tell myself who and what I am, then no one can. I\u2019ve always been able to look in the mirror and say all right, this is where you are, this is where you are, this is where you are. And try to put myself in a position where I get the best out of where I am, and also the team that I play for gets the best of what I am. Coming back to Miami at this time, for me, it\u2019s selfless, man. I just want to help this team win, and I don\u2019t really care about how many minutes I play right now, how many shots I get. I\u2019m just taking what the game gives me. Last night, nine shots. Tonight, probably more. So each game is different and I\u2019m trying to do whatever I can each game to help this team get to the point where they\u2019re in the playoffs. Me: Whatever needs doing that night? DW: Whatever needs doing that night. That\u2019s what my dad told me. When I first started playing basketball, my dad was the first one who was never, never patted me on the back when I scored 30. He always wanted me to be a complete player; he always wanted me to be a team player. I didn\u2019t get it early on, and as I got to high school, my high school coach was the same way. My college coach was the same way. So I\u2019ve always been, whatever the team needs, I play a team sport. I don\u2019t play an individual sport. And you can still get everything you want in the midst of that. You can still star in it. It hasn\u2019t changed anything in my life, coming off the bench, at all. I understand that. Me: How important was it for you that your kids got to see you in that uniform again? DW: I think especially for my oldest son, Zaire, I think it was great. Obviously me being home, but it\u2019s just like everyone else \u2014 I look right to him in a Heat jersey. He\u2019s getting older, he\u2019s 16 years old, and he got to feel the love. My youngest son Zion, he gets to feel the love of the city. They come home and we\u2019re talking, and he says \u2018dad, everyone\u2019s so happy you\u2019re back.\u2019 It has to feel good for them as well. DW: Something\u2019s going on in the world, and it speaks to you. You try to get involved and you try to get into it. When something happens in your backyard, it\u2019s even tougher. This situation happened, and you start hearing all the stories from all the kids. From a basketball side, and being fans of mine and the Miami Heat, the only thing we can do for the families is to honor them \u2014 obviously by the way we play, but honoring the school. Putting the hashtag on my shoes. Honoring a young man who was buried in my jersey. Continuing to make sure that the kids who was lost, that their legacy in life continues to live on. Just keep talking about them, moreso than the one who took their lives. Let\u2019s not put the light on him; let\u2019s put it on the 17 young lives that was lost. It\u2019s definitely, myself and Udonis (Haslem) and other guys that\u2019s been trying to do things to impact the families, try to impact the community as much as possible during this tough time. It\u2019s our duty, I feel, as public figures and figureheads in the state to do that. Me: And not shut up and dribble. DW: Definitely. We\u2019re never going to do that. I love athletes today, nowadays, feeling confident to step up and stand out, and talk about things that they believe in. We\u2019re, we might make a lot of money, we might be on TV, we might be looked at, but we\u2019re part of the community. We hear the community. We hear what everybody\u2019s talking about. And we understand that their voices might not be heard the way our voices would be heard. Like I said, it\u2019s my duty to speak for them as well. We might not have the same challenges that our community is having, but it\u2019s our job to not only give back and do things that we do, but also be the voice and be the face for our community. From Li Rui: Lottery Reform, Part the Thousandth. From Jimmy Chow: Lottery Reform, Part Infinity. From Roland Beech: Let me take the last first. Roland, for those who don\u2019t know, is a legend in the analytics game, the founder of the website 82games.com who was thought of so much he has had subsequently VP jobs with both the Mavericks and the Kings in recent years. And as evidenced by his Fighting Loss stat, he keeps on top of these kinds of things. (Thank you for writing, Roland!) It should thus not surprise that of all these ideas I think his is the best and most equitable. Returning to the simple, one ball\/card per Lottery team system, which the NBA used until 1990, when it went to the weighted system to give the team with the worst record the best chance to get the first pick, would also be my preference for fixing the current system. As Roland wrote, the beauty of a system like this is that it would fully democratize the Lottery and give all teams in all situations a chance to get better fast. For the sake of argument, let\u2019s say New Orleans stumbles down the stretch and misses the playoffs by a game or two. How great would it be for the future of basketball there if the Pelicans got the first-or second-pick and could add a great young player to team with Anthony Davis, Jrue Holiday and\/or DeMarcus Cousins if he re-signs? But it would also provide immediate help to the Phoenixes and Sacramentos of the league if they were so lucky. Most importantly, as Roland wrote, going back to the old system would eliminate any benefit from tanking \u2014 and, thus, would likely end most of it around the league. Which is the best possible outcome for everyone. We discuss guns. Again. From Scott Young: Well, let\u2019s unpack all of that, Scott. First, I do not assume that any discussion about banning certain types of guns (or, in our country, banning anything) would or should be \u201ceasy.\u201d I would cite in response the quote attributed by most to Albert Einstein: \u201cwhat is popular is not always right, and what is right is not always popular.\u201d Getting Civil Rights and Voting Rights and Housing Rights for African-Americans was not \u201ceasy.\u201d Nor was it \u201ceasy\u201d to get women the vote. Nor was it \u201ceasy\u201d to get same-sex marriage rights. But, in all of those cases, it was the right thing to do. So is common sense gun regulation. The \u201ctyranny\u201d argument supporting the availability of all weapons for everyone is, as you no doubt know, one that has been twisted into grotesque caricature over the last few decades by a relentless push from the right wing of the American body politic, financed in large part and fanned rhetorically by the National Rifle Association (which is, by the way, its complete right to do; our country is based on the notion that if you can get enough people to agree with your position on anything, you can try and get it codified into law). But that doesn\u2019t mean that I have to agree with that push. Warren Burger, the former Chief Justice of the Supreme Court appointed by Richard Nixon \u2014 he, no liberal \u2014 famously said in 1991 that the Second Amendment \u201chas been the subject of one of the greatest pieces of fraud \u2014 I repeat the word \u2018fraud\u2019 \u2014 on the American public by special interest groups that I have ever seen in my lifetime.\u201d https:\/\/www.youtube.com\/watch?v Even the late Antonin Scalia \u2014 he, also, no liberal \u2014 wrote, in the case that actually established, in the Supreme Court\u2019s view, a Constitutional right of citizens to carry weapons in 2008, Heller vs. District of Columbia , that there were limits to the Second Amendment : We also recognize another important limitation on the right to keep and carry arms. \u2018Miller\u2019 said, as we have explained, that the sorts of weapons protected were those \u2018in common use at the time.\u2019 307 U.S., at 179, 59 S.Ct. 816. We think that limitation is fairly supported by the historical tradition of prohibiting the carrying of \u2018dangerous and unusual weapons.\u2019 I would argue that the AR-15 is a \u201cdangerous and unusual weapon.\u201d And, while many cite the example of Supreme Court Justice Oliver Wendell Holmes in Schenk vs. United States in 1919 that a person cannot falsely shout \u201cfire\u201d in a crowded theatre as evidence of the limits of the First Amendment protection regarding free speech (Schenk, a socialist, had been arrested and charged under the Espionage Act, whose limits on speech during wartime were upheld by the Court), the Court\u2019s current position on First Amendment limits was set in a case where, ironically, the Court overturned the conviction of a Klansman for speech that had initially been viewed as advocating violence. The case, Brandenburg vs. Ohio , was heard in 1969. Brandenburg, a Klan leader in Ohio, had invited a reporter to come to a Klan rally in 1964 in which the usual things about black people and Jews were said. Brandenburg spoke at the rally and said the usual things Klansmen say about the government being on the side of blacks and Jews and against the white man. But because the reporter was there and wrote down the things Brandenburg said, he was subsequently arrested and ultimately convicted under Ohio\u2019s Criminal Syndicalism statute, that state\u2019s version of a law passed by many states after World War I, most notably California. That law forbid \u201cany doctrine or precept advocating, teaching or aiding and abetting the commission of crime, sabotage (which word is hereby defined as meaning willful and malicious physical damage or injury to physical property), or unlawful acts of force and violence or unlawful methods of terrorism as a means of accomplishing a change in industrial ownership or control or effecting any political change.\u201d Brandenburg hired the ACLU (!) to appeal his conviction after subsequent Ohio courts had upheld it, and in \u201969, the Supreme Court overturned his conviction \u2014 and in doing so, overturned much of the precedent set in the Schenk decision. The Court held that abstract discussion of violating laws, as Brandenburg had done in his speech, could not be subsequently punished by government \u2014 with one exception: Freedoms of speech and press do not permit a State to forbid advocacy of the use of force or of law violation except where such advocacy is directed to inciting or producing imminent lawless action and is likely to incite or produce such action. (emphasis mine) The point is that there are , in some cases, limits to free speech, and thus, limits to even the First Amendment \u2014 of which journalists, especially, are quite protective. And, as Scalia wrote in supporting Heller, there are limits to the Second Amendment as well. (There are limits to just about all the Amendments; police cannot enter your home and search it, as enumerated in the Fourth Amendment \u2014 unless they have probable cause and can get a warrant from a judge to do so.) I would not support any law or law enforcement body that tries to take away legally purchased handguns (and I would include in this automatic\/semiautomatic handguns like the Luger, Glock, Beretta, etc., that not only are used for home defense and sport, but also have historic and\/or retail value). I would not support any law or law enforcement body that tries to take away legally purchased rifles (but not knockoffs of the M-16, like the AR-15, or other military weapons). I would not support any law or law enforcement body that tries to take away legally purchased shotguns (I am willing to listen on the arguments about why you\u2019d need semiautomatic\/automatic shotguns). I think any of those types of guns, used individually or in tandem by people who have been properly trained to use them, are more than adequate enough for home defense, and certainly should be free to be used for hunting, target shooting and the like. I draw the line at guns like the AR-15, that are not, as much as you or others may argue, designed for hunting, or home defense, or anything of the sort. They are designed to kill as many human beings as possible in as short an amount of time as possible. They are weapons of war, which were designed to be used on a battlefield, not to be purchased by teenagers or the mentally ill. Fox News contributor Retired Lt. Col. Ralph Peters \u2014 he, he writes for the third time, no liberal \u2014 just wrote that these types of weapons should be banned from civilian use . I support a ban on the AR-15. I also support slowing down the process of legally purchasing other guns. The reasoning is the same as it would be for someone who wants to buy a car, or a house, or who wants to become a lawyer, or a doctor. No one says \u2018why can\u2019t I become a doctor in three days?\u2019 We all understand that such an important undertaking requires that you demonstrate, over a period of time, that you are competent and trustworthy enough to handle the job. We all understand that buying a car requires you to demonstrate that you can drive well enough to use one, that you have enough money to buy one and can make the payments, and that you can purchase insurance to protect yourself and others in case of an accident. Buying a gun should require, at the least, similar demonstrations of fitness. In Japan, it takes four months to get a gun , during which time prospective buyers have to take classes in gun ownership and use, visit their local police, and are thoroughly investigated to see if there are any red flags in their background, if they can properly lock and store the weapon(s) in their homes, and the like. And then, they get their gun. I applied for the Global Entry program a few years ago, which allows for expedited security through airports around the world. I had to meet with and be interviewed by law enforcement. I had to wait a few weeks so that they could check me out. Which they did. Eventually, I got my Global Entry card. I did not ask, \u2018why can\u2019t I get my card in 48 hours?\u2019 If you are a law-abiding citizen with nothing to hide in your past, why would that kind of procedure to get a gun be a problem for you? And before you ask, no, it would not catch every crazy person who wanted to buy a gun. But it would catch more of them. Similarly, a ban on the AR-15 and similar types guns would not end all mass shootings. It would not end all killings associated with domestic violence. It would certainly not stop all crime. No sentient person would argue the contrary. My argument is that we have to start somewhere. There is a reason the most of the most recent mass murderers used this particular gun. No, it wouldn\u2019t stop anyone from stabbing death, or getting killed by getting hit in the head with a shovel. When we have a spike in mass stabbing\/shovel deaths, perhaps I will rethink my position. Did it count double what some ordinary contest might have? Triple? Keep going. More like exponential. It\u2019s too early to claim that Minnesota\u2019s resiliency in the comeback from 12 points down, against the defending champ, saved their season. But the dueling scenarios, win vs. lose, were rather stark for a team facing a rigorous and largely uncharted final month. Fail Sunday, and the Wolves would be lugging a four-game skid on the road to face Washington Tuesday and San Antonio Saturday. By the time they got home to face Houston Sunday night, the losing streak could be six, going on seven. The Timberwolves at the All-Star break was a surprising third seed in the West. However, since Jimmy Butler\u2019s absence from the lineup after a right meniscus tear on Feb. 23, the Wolves have gone 2-4. Now the Wolves, whether they admit it publicly or not, are driven simply to qualify. Period. Ending up seventh or eighth is no prize, given a likely first-round ordeal against either the Rockets or the Warriors. But for a franchise that hasn\u2019t reached the postseason since 2004, either would be far better than landing ninth. By beating the Warriors, though, the Wolves bought themselves time and opened a smidgen of breathing room over the next few days. More than that, they responded to a serious challenge the way a playoff wannabe is supposed to. They didn\u2019t unravel, they stuck to what was working and they had players slide into Butler\u2019s roles as primary defender, go-to scorer and late-game closer. That is essential until the All-Star wing and obvious team leader returns, ideally, for playoffs that his teammates can deliver. Center Karl-Anthony Towns scored 14 of his team-high 31 points in the fourth quarter. Wing Andrew Wiggins scored 22 of his 23 in the first three quarters to help Minnesota claw back to an 84-84 tie. Those two stepping into the void of Butler\u2019s injury suggested the sort of growth that, frankly, coach Tom Thibodeau and the team\u2019s followers might look back on after this season (and postseason?) as a turning point. \u201cThis is a great opportunity for everybody, and certainly those two, in that whenever you have someone like Jimmy go out, it\u2019s an opportunity to grow and get experience in different situations,\u201d Thibodeau said. \u201cWe\u2019ve talked about it a lot. We have good veterans on the team. But this is an opportunity for them to step up and lead.\u201d Sure, Golden State was playing without team MVP Steph Curry (ankle) and ace reserve Andre Iguodala (wrist). But the visitors still had three All-Stars and the motivation of Friday\u2019s loss in Portland to propel them through the matinee. Towns and Wiggins, both still 22 years old, stayed cool in reacting and thwarting Golden State double-teams. He came in averaging just 3.2 field-goal attempts in the fourth quarter this season, with 1.8 buckets and 5.1 points. Compare that to his 5.7 makes, 10.6 shots and 15.4 scoring averages through the first three quarters of games so far this season. His usage rate drops from 22.4 to 20.9 when it ought to go up. You\u2019d believe that too if you saw his work in the final three minutes, from bulling through Draymond Green for a layup that made it 101-96 to stepping in for a left baseline jumper two possessions later. At 104-103, Towns posted up Green near the end line again, banged a bit, then spun for a fadeaway jumper. Next time down, he followed up a shot against Durant to all but clinch it. The play of Towns, Wiggins and the other three Minnesota starters took any onus off Derrick Rose. Newly signed by his old Chicago coach, Rose had a rusty, regrettable debut with the Wolves, missing five of his six shots with two turnovers and a minus-17 in just 6:36. But his presence, if nothing else, ought to remind Towns and Wiggins that 22 is plenty old enough to grab a pack of Wolves by the scruff of their necks and take responsibility. Rose was 22 when he became the youngest MVP in NBA history, leading the Bulls all the way to the Eastern Conference finals that season. Minnesota basically is in the playoffs now \u2013 every outcome matters, bolstering or damaging its run to the postseason. There\u2019s no running away now, no hiding either. \u201cI think we\u2019re more prepared because we\u2019ve had most of the season to go through experiences,\u201d Towns said. \u201cNow that we\u2019re at this point, we have the chance to do something great. It\u2019s for us as a group to take all the experiences we\u2019ve had \u2013 of losing close games, winning big, winning games offensively, winning games defensively \u2013 and putting them to [use].\u201d It is vital that the Wolves\u2019 young stars stay focused on the opportunities before them, rather than succumbing to the pressure. Said Towns: \u201cThe thing is, you don\u2019t ever want to have pressure turn to stress. We have to make sure we keep our composure. Obviously, the situation we\u2019re in, it\u2019s a lot of pressure on us. But we can\u2019t turn that into stress, because that\u2019s when we start becoming undisciplined and start making errors that are more mental.\u201d The proof now is in the playing, said Thibodeau. \u201cThe best leadership you can have is your actions,\u201d the coach said. \u201cWhat are you doing? It\u2019s one of Tom Thibodeau\u2019s most familiar exhortations, a mantra of sorts to keep his teams locked in, digging down and generally committed through whatever grueling test they\u2019re facing, be it a game, a road trip, a spate of injuries or the entire season. The trouble for Derrick Rose with that particular Thibs-ism is, so often, he has been the rope. On one side of an unfortunate tug o\u2019 war, we\u2019ve had the Rose loyalists, the fans, friends and family who believe that the 2010-11 NBA Most Valuable Player\u2019s return from injury hell to elite status is just one more, legit opportunity away. Pulling from the other side, there is a growing group of Rose skeptics who are convinced that the Chicago kid\u2019s best days \u2013 his most explosive, elusive, game-changing moves \u2013 are behind him, strewn on the floors of too many surgical rooms and rehab gyms. Rose, 29, knows they\u2019re there. One group pulling for him, the other doubting him. And in an unusually candid and forceful moment Saturday, the normally soft-spoken Rose delivered a stark message to them all. \u201cYeah,\u201d Rose said after his first full practice since signing a minimum-salary contract Thursday to join the Minnesota Timberwolves. \u201cThis is how I feel about the whole perspective on it: You can have your perspective on me as far as I\u2019m a bum, I can\u2019t play, I can\u2019t shoot, this and that. All right. Cool. I have no hard feelings with that. I\u2019m cool with that. If that\u2019s how you feel, that\u2019s how you feel. \u201cBut at the same time, I don\u2019t need your [bleeping] validation.\u201d Rose\u2019s eyes burned bright, in a direct response to the many health challenges he has endured from acquaintances and strangers both, picking at whatever good or bad is left of his basketball career. \u201cI know who I am,\u201d Rose continued. \u201cI know the type of player I am. So, you respect that and I respect that, and we should be good. That\u2019s how I feel about it.\u201d In other words, you work your side of the street, Rose will continue to work his. If there are NBA administrators like Thibodeau, the Wolves\u2019 head coach and president of basketball operations, willing to give him another chance, he\u2019ll be chasing the ghost of his own self while trying to help somebody win. Rose\u2019s latest grab at faded glory began in Sunday\u2019s matinee against the defending champion Golden State Warriors at Target Center, where the Timberwolves beat the Warriors, 109-103 . Rose scored two points on 1-for-5 shooting from the field in his Wolves debut. It probably is his last, best shot to salvage something from a 2017-18 season that\u2019s been largely lost due to injury, yes, but other factors outside Rose\u2019s control as well. What looked like a terrific opportunity back in training camp \u2013 signing with Eastern Conference power Cleveland Cavaliers and home to the game\u2019s best player (and Rose nemesis) in LeBron James \u2013 got sideways fast. In the Cavs\u2019 second game, on a drive to the rim, Rose got whacked across the face and neck by Milwaukee center Greg Monroe. Then word got out just before Thanksgiving that Rose had left the team, reportedly to contemplate his future as an NBA player. He was gone for nearly two weeks, at least part of it back home in Chicago, during what Cavs GM Koby Altman called \u201ca very challenging and difficult time for Derrick.\u201d Rose didn\u2019t play again until Cleveland\u2019s 44th game. In nine appearances over the next three weeks, he was a shell of the three-time All-Star he\u2019d once been, averaging 6.3 points, 1.6 assists and 13.3 minutes, while shooting 39 percent. On Feb. 8, he was one of six Cavaliers players dealt by Altman at the NBA trade deadline, sent to Salt Lake City as a throw-in to acquire Utah\u2019s Rodney Hood and Sacramento\u2019s George Hill. Two days later, the Jazz waived Rose . Four weeks passed before Thibodeau got the green light from Minnesota owner Glen Taylor to sign Rose. The Oklahoma City Thunder had sniffed in his direction, only to opt for veteran backup Corey Brewer. Rose had family duties to attend to \u2013 he and Alaina Anderson had a baby girl in Chicago to start the week \u2013 but he also had spent time working out by himself in the Cavs\u2019 facility or at Cleveland State\u2019s gym. The end seemed near. Given Rose\u2019s limited involvement this season, he probably would have been a long shot to land with one of the league\u2019s 30 teams in 2018-19, had Thibodeau not reached out. The people on the dark end of Rose\u2019s rope were winning. Now, this buys him time for a shout-out to the folks on the other end. \u201c\u2018Don\u2019t give up,\u2019 Rose said he would tell them. Talking later at the downtown Minneapolis hotel where he\u2019s staying, he wanted to assure people that his desire to play remains strong, his passion to keep trying still burns, and his mental fitness for this and future challenges on or away from the court is fine. \u201cI still have faith,\u201d Rose said, two bags of ice strapped to each leg. \u201cNo matter what happens, I still have a lot of faith in myself and my ability. It\u2019s just about opportunity and catching a rhythm. Whenever I do catch a rhythm, I\u2019d rather see what it is then. Than to, like, give up knowing I have so much left. Like, \u2018Damn, I should have kept playing.\u2019 \u201cI\u2019m going to give it my all. And once I do, then it\u2019s like, \u2018All right, cool. I gave it my all, now what\u2019s this next phase in my life?\u2019 \u201cBut as far as right now, I\u2019m still in it. I\u2019ve got two kids that can look at me now. The oldest, my boy [P.J.] is 5 years old. He\u2019s looking at me right now. He sees everything. I\u2019m going to tell him, \u2018No excuses. Don\u2019t come to me cryin\u2019, this and that. Nah.\u2019 He\u2019ll see what I\u2019ve had to go through. \u2018Now suck it up and go out there and do what you\u2019ve got to do.\u2019\u201d For some NBA players whose careers got waylaid by injuries \u2013 Brandon Roy, Greg Oden, Penny Hardaway \u2013 their bodies finally refused to cooperate. They went from 60-to-0, no wiggle room on whether they would continue. Rose, for all his setbacks, has worked his way back \u2013 not back to his previous form \u2013 from each and every injury. From the ACL blowout that started him down his hobbled path in April 2012 to three subsequent meniscus knee surgeries, from the left orbital fracture he suffered when he caught teammate Taj Gibson\u2019s errant elbow in the face in the opening practice of 2015-16 to the lingering ankle sprain dealt by Monroe\u2019s blow in October. In that sense, Rose is more like Bernard King, Sam Bowie or Grant Hill, standout players whose career trajectories were forever altered \u2013 but not ended \u2013 by injuries. Rose speaks as if he has reached some level of peace with his maladies, referring to his injuries as \u201cpart of the game\u201d and his particular \u201ccross\u201d to bear. \u201cI\u2019ve just had five surgeries more than other people,\u201d he said. \u201cThat\u2019s the way I look at it. That don\u2019t mean that I can\u2019t play. That don\u2019t mean that I lost my love for the game. No.\u201d What Rose doesn\u2019t like is the \u201cfragile\u201d label that\u2019s been affixed to him. He\u2019s less interested that he has played in only 486 of approximately 789 regular-season games so far, while proud of the 130 he logged with the Bulls (2015-16) and Knicks (2016-17) more recently. It seems clear that the reckless abandon with which Rose played \u2013 and the excruciating torque he put on his knees with his bounding, zig-zag attacks through the lane \u2013 wreaked havoc on his knees. Beyond that, though, he\u2019s not buying any pattern business. \u201cYou see how I was injured [in October]? I was taken out of the air,\u201d Rose said. \u201cPeople are like, \u2018Aw, he\u2019s always injured.\u2019 Are you just watching highlights, just looking at clips, like new fans are these days? Or are you watching an entire game? Are you just reading reports that come up on your phone?\u201d Scouts say that Rose has lost both quickness and leaping ability, without developing a perimeter game to compensate. They also bundle his Cleveland hiatus with the AWOL episode last season with the Knicks , when Rose left the team without notice before a game against New Orleans, to question his reliability and commitment. Rose disputes the comments about his game, citing the circumstances in New York and Cleveland. \u201cI could sit here and tell you, \u2018I\u2019m gonna try to change this. Do this and do that.\u2019 Nah, I always felt, it starts with my rhythm,\u201d he said. \u201c[In] New York \u2026 I was playing the triangle [offense favored by former Knicks president Phil Jackson] and still playing pretty well [18.0 ppg, 4.4 apg, 32.5 mpg]. In Cleveland, when did I really have a chance to catch a rhythm? When did I play 20 games straight? Or 10 games? Five games?\u201d As for his reliability \u2013 or likelihood to take a powder on the Wolves the way he did on the Knicks and seemed to do on the Cavs \u2013 Rose said there is no issue there, either. In the past couple weeks, Toronto\u2019s DeMar DeRozan (depression) and Cleveland\u2019s Kevin Love ( panic attacks ) have opened up about psychological challenges they and other athletes face. But Rose shook his head as the question was asked. \u201cOh no, no, no,\u201d he said. \u201cI\u2019m blessed, man. Beyond blessed. It\u2019s not even \u2026 what do I have to complain about? I don\u2019t have anything to complain about. Of course, I wish I was on the court more. I think in time, with the right opportunity, I\u2019ll be out there more. \u201cI\u2019m not depressed, even though I think everybody deals with some depression in some way. It\u2019s about how you deal with it. We\u2019re emotional creatures. We hold onto things. I try to meditate, try to do little things to change my mindset and try to read things to easy my nerves.\u201d Rose admitted he did wonder if he would get another chance, once the Cavs traded him to a Jazz team that had no use for him. \u201cEspecially when you get dropped by a team like Cleveland, that needed players,\u201d he said. \u201cIt makes other teams think, \u2018Damn, if they didn\u2019t keep him\u2026\u2019\u201d Rose has not spoken with James since being dealt, he said. \u201cThe way I take it, I don\u2019t take it as personal,\u201d Rose said. \u201cThey didn\u2019t need my services. That\u2019s the way I look at it, OK? I understand. It\u2019s business. Does that stop me from working hard? Does that stop me from still putting out goals and trying to reach my goals? No.\u201d Now Rose is reunited with Thibodeau, Gibson, Jimmy Butler (sidelined after his own meniscus surgery) and familiar coaches and staff making up the \u201cTimberBulls.\u201d He even trusts Thibodeau, often criticized for the heavy minutes he loads on his top players, not to break him. \u201cIf anything, I want him to play me,\u201d Rose said. \u201cI want to show to him that I can still play. I want him to see me and be like, \u2018Damn, he\u2019s still got it.\u2019 I want him to count on me. I want to be held accountable. You know what I mean? I don\u2019t just want to be, like, an average guy on the team riding along just to see how far they go. I really want to add.\u201d Said Thibodeau, who ran Rose Saturday through a rigorous refresher course on his playbook: \u201cObviously when he was at an MVP level, that was the peak. But he also, my last year in Chicago, he had a great year. \u2026 He still has the potential to be very good. He\u2019s young, that was the other part of it. He knows some of our guys, he knows the system. \u201cLike all stories, there\u2019s a beginning, there\u2019s a middle and there\u2019s an end,\u201d the Wolves coach added. \u201cI don\u2019t think it\u2019s a finished story.\u201d Gibson thinks Rose can shoulder some of Butler\u2019s late-game duties, simply because the scoring guard has strong muscle memory of such situations. He, too, hopes Rose\u2019s story can take a happy turn. \u201cI\u2019ve got my fingers crossed,\u201d the veteran forward said. \u201cI truly believe in him. He\u2019s got a lot left in the tank. It\u2019s just, sometimes life doesn\u2019t go your way and you have to push through it and keep fighting.\u201d Thibodeau has said that Rose, like starter Jeff Teague and backup Tyus Jones, can play both backcourt spots, so he can mix-and-match based on situations. Rose anticipates no problem walking that line between asserting his game and rocking the Wolves\u2019 boat. \u201cMy job coming here, I\u2019m not trying to step on nobody\u2019s toes. I\u2019m not trying to take someone\u2019s spot,\u201d he said. \u201cI\u2019m not trying to show myself. Nah. I\u2019m here to win. Me going out there and playing, hopefully you all see that. \u2018He\u2019s making money plays. He\u2019s playing to win. And that\u2019s what we wanted from him.\u2019\u201d Not that Rose, lest we forget from up top, needs anyone\u2019s bleeping validation. Boosters and doubters can pull this way or that, but he said he\u2019ll be the one who decides when his time is up. \u201cWhen my love of the game is not there,\u201d Rose said, sounding sincere near the end of his 10th season overall. \u201cWhen I get tired of going to the gym. \u201cDon\u2019t get me wrong, we all go through that. But after a couple of days, I get antsy, I want to be in the gym. When a week or two goes by and I haven\u2019t touched the gym, even in the summer, oh yeah, I\u2019d know it was over.\u201d Steve Aschburner has written about the NBA since 1980. He does. He\u2019s been on an absolute tear since Feb. 14, playing perhaps the best basketball of his career while averaging 33.1 points, 5.8 assists, 4.1 rebounds and 1.3 steals per game to push and shove his way into the MVP conversation in the final weeks of the race. Lillard\u2019s best and most compelling statement, however, might be the Trail Blazers\u2019 rise from the pack in a crowded Western Conference playoff chase into the top three heading into Friday night\u2019s win over the Golden State Warriors . Lillard, an Oakland native, popped his hometown team for 44 points and eight assists in a 123-117 win on Valentine\u2019s Day and the Trail Blazers haven\u2019t lost since, rattling off nine straight to join a group of elite teams in the top four of the Western Conference standings \u2014 the Houston Rockets, Warriors and New Orleans Pelicans \u2014 that had combined for a mind-boggling 42 straight wins between them. That the Trail Blazers are included in the group is remarkable, given their 2-4 start to February prior to that pivotal win over the Warriors.
