The views on this page do not necessarily reflect the views of the NBA, its clubs or Turner Broadcasting."},{"id":41485,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/state-nba-adam-silver-memo-tanking-2017-18-mark-cuban-fined-600000","date":"2018-03-05","title":"Mark Cuban's tanking talk (and NBA-issued fine) speaks of larger issues in league","content":" The NBA fined Dallas Mavericks owner Mark Cuban $600,000 for being honest. Cuban told Naismith Memorial Hall of Famer Julius Erving on Erving\u2019s podcast a couple of weeks ago that he told his players during a recent dinner that \u201closing is our best option. Adam (Silver) would hate hearing that\u2026(but) we want the players to understand. As a player, you know that even though you may not agree, but at least if you respect the fact that someone took the time to talk to you, and you understood their perspective, you\u2019re going to give me your feedback, but you\u2019re part of the process.\u201d But the league fined Cuban for what it called \u201cpublic statements detrimental to the NBA\u201d three days later. And Silver sent a memo to all 30 teams last week detailing the league\u2019s position. *  Official release on Cuban\u2019s fine \u201cThroughout this period,\u201d Silver wrote, \u201cwe have been careful to distinguish between efforts teams may make to rebuild their rosters, including through personnel changes over the course of several seasons, and circumstances in which players or coaches on the floor take steps to lose games. \u201cThe former can be a legitimate strategy to construct a successful team within the confines of league rules; the latter \u2014 which we have not found and hope never to see in the NBA \u2014 has no place in our game.\u201d https:\/\/art19.com\/shows\/house-call-with-dr-j\/episodes\/069d0067-5793-4bf2-8461-3f92c4c307f9%20 Yet Cuban did not in any way, nor has any evidence to the contrary emerged, state the Mavericks were losing games on purpose; that is, players were intentionally missing shots, or not putting forth effort on defense to let the other team score, or anything like that. (Even Silver acknowledged in the memo that the league has \u201cno basis at this time to conclude that the Mavericks team is giving anything less than its best effort on the court, and Mark has assured us that this is not the case.\u201d) So, why the fine? Was what Cuban said so incendiary? \u2018\u2019Mark knew his comments were public, so it surprised me that he was so candid, but that\u2019s who Mark is,\u201d said one very high-ranking official from another team over the weekend. \u201cTo me his comment wasn\u2019t indicating tanking as their strategy but more about setting the expectation that playoffs were not a possibility. The only consolation of not making the playoffs is being in the lottery. You can\u2019t blame a team from trying to turn the lemon (losing) into lemonade (top 4 pick). The league needs to find a way not to reward losing.\u201d Exactly. What Cuban said was spot on \u2014 losing to improve the Mavericks\u2019 Draft position was, and is, the best and quickest way for Dallas to get better and start winning games again. That doesn\u2019t mean everyone agreed with Cuban being so blunt. \u201cI think it was a totally inappropriate to say that to players,\u201d said another extremely high-ranking team official for another team. \u201cWhatever the team\u2019s strategy may be, I firmly believe that the players should always play to win. The fine is meaningless to Mark; in fact, sometimes I think he enjoys the publicity he gets from the fines.\u201d But. We ask people to be truthful and not lie about their intentions. We tell our kids that no lie is worth telling, and that telling the truth, no matter how painful, is always the best choice. So Cuban is honest and tells the truth, that short-term losing makes more sense for his franchise\u2019s long-term interests, and he\u2019s relieved of 600 large by the league. Meanwhile, the Philadelphia 76ers are lauded \u2014 and revel in their slogan, \u201cTrust the Process,\u201d celebrated by the team\u2019s most ardent supporters \u2014 whose central tenet was to lose , and keep losing , until you could draft a player good enough to build around and win down the road . Which is, exactly, what Dallas is doing now. Indeed, increased tanking is the logical extension of an analytics-dominant league. If three is greater than two \u2014 the reasoning behind the primacy of the 3-pointer in today\u2019s NBA \u2014 then doing anything you can to get more ping-pong balls in the hopper is the correct thing to do. You can\u2019t just embrace the parts of doing it by the numbers that are pleasant. This is the flip side. Burying one\u2019s head in the sand and pretending teams don\u2019t do this doesn\u2019t make sense. Everyone does it in every sport, or don\u2019t you recall \u201cSuck for Luck,\u201d the chant of Indianapolis Colts\u2019 fans before the 2012 NFL Draft? What of Major League Baseball\u2019s Houston Astros losing 324 games from 2011-13? Were they trying to win games, or did we all imagine them going from $102 million in payroll in 2009 to $26 million by 2013? \u201cI resist the word \u2018tanking,\u2019 but I\u2019m very pro \u2018rebuilding,\u2019 when it\u2019s necessary,\u201d said Los Angeles Dodgers President Stan Kasten, who in a former life ran the Hawks as general manager in the \u201880s and \u201890s, by telephone Sunday. \u201cAnd, it\u2019s painful,\u201d Kasten said. \u201cYou\u2019ve got to explain it to your team, your fans, to your front office, to your coaches, to your wife, to your kids, to the country club. It\u2019s hard. It\u2019s painful. It\u2019s nobody\u2019s first choice. But if it\u2019s necessary, it\u2019s often the quickest way to get the team back to winning. And don\u2019t lose sight of that.\u201d Kasten\u2019s Dodgers lost the World Series to the Astros , who methodically built their team the last four years around young drafted players like Series MVP George Springer , last fall in seven games. But not only is he not angry with Houston for the way management took the franchise\u2019s foundation to the studs \u2014 compared with his high-spending Dodgers \u2014 he admires the speed with which they went from worst to first. \u201cI have real feelings about what they did,\u201d Kasten said. \u201cBecause Mark Walter (the CEO of Guggenheim Partners, the global firm that bought the Dodgers in 2012 ) and I, before we bought the Dodgers, we were looking at Houston. Because they were available. And truthfully, when we looked at where they were, we were going to do the same thing. It had to be done. Because they were not on a track to win. And frankly, I don\u2019t think I could have done it as fast, or as well, as (Astros owner) Jim Crane, or (GM) Jeff Luhnow. Because doing that, to the extreme, takes real intestinal fortitude.\u201d  Kasten makes a strong distinction between a team cutting payroll and going young and that winds up losing, and one that\u2019s actively seeking ways to lose more games. \u201cAll of these owners are hyper-competitive, and they want to win,\u201d Kasten said. \u201cAnd truthfully, the quickest way to win, at least if you look at the last three world champions, is to rebuild and get young and get prospects and do it that way. And if you don\u2019t think that\u2019s the better way to go, ask the fans in Houston and Chicago and Kansas City how they feel. You won\u2019t get one fan who disagrees with what is done. It is the quickest way to win.\u201d Please do not misunderstand. I hate tanking. I hate the idea of introducing losing into your shop, even indirectly. It\u2019s like a virus, extremely difficult to get rid of once it gets in a franchise\u2019s bloodstream. A ticket is, in essence, a contract between parties: I pay top dollar, you give me top-dollar product in exchange. When a team tanks, it violates that compact; I don\u2019t recall any team that\u2019s given fans a tanking discount. It is also very difficult to tank effectively in the NBA. The last three teams with the best odds of getting the No. 1 in the Draft going into the Lottery \u2014 Boston (2017), Philadelphia (2016) and Minnesota (2015) \u2014 have indeed won. But prior to that, the team with the best odds didn\u2019t get the first pick for 10 consecutive years, and 22 times out of the last 25 years. And even the teams that did buck the odds and get the first pick often picked wrong, or did I miss Anthony Bennett Night in Cleveland, or the Andrea Bargnani statue outside of Air Canada Centre? \u201cThe Draft is often a crap shoot anyway,\u201d the official from the second team said. \u201cSo why not give your fans the best product that you can and then draft Donovan Mitchell,\u201d as Utah did this season. The Jazz traded for the rights to the Kia Rookie of the Year candidate , who was taken near the bottom of the Lottery (13th overall by the Denver Nuggets). This came a season after the Jazz went 51-31 and won its first-round playoff series . I agree. Tanking does not reward excellence in team building \u2014 good drafting, good free-agent signings, good player development \u2014 it rewards the exact opposite of that. It\u2019s a Golden Ticket that doesn\u2019t even require you to buy an Everlasting Gobstopper. But, tanking is reality. You can\u2019t pretend it isn\u2019t. And the only way to completely get tanking out of pro sports is to eliminate the Draft in all sports, including the NBA. We don\u2019t want to have that conversation, do we? Personally, I\u2019d love it. Can you imagine the fight that would set up between interested teams \u2014 and who wouldn\u2019t be interested? \u2014 in a certain 7-foot-1 freshman center almost certain to leave school early who currently plays for a school that\u2019s been in the news for all the wrong reasons lately? Would he help the Lakers? The Knicks? The Bulls? The NBA team in the state in which the college player currently plays, which rather desperately needs another star to pair with its one really great player (whose name, if you must know, rhymes with \u201cNevin Cooker\u201d)? Would he help any team in the league that doesn\u2019t currently employ Anthony Davis, Joel Embiid or Karl-Anthony Towns in the middle? Most assuredly. And if he could control where he wanted to go, and for how much, the process would be must-see TV. Yet, while the real-world implications would be fascinating, I\u2019m not sure how you could eliminate the Draft without loosening the underpinnings of the entire pro basketball enterprise (and, yes, one could make a moral case for doing just that, as it does go against the whole Manifest Destiny thing to artificially bind someone to a company rather than letting them market their services to the highest bidder). If there was no Draft, why would any player with Lottery-level talent go to college? Yes, there would be the occasional Grant Hill\/unicorn who wants to go to college to better themselves intellectually and\/or embrace the person growth that often comes from being on your own for four years. But, while sad to say, most kids with NBA dreams go to college because that\u2019s the path through which they can ultimately get to the pros the fastest. With no Draft, and few of the top college-age players thus needing\/wanting to go to college, you\u2019d have a very different March Madness than you have now. And as that is a multi-billion enterprise, both for the broadcast networks that air it (including Turner Sports, which runs NBA.com) and the colleges that reap the financial deluge it produces, the likelihood of across the board support for a new player acquisition model is slight. Not to mention, you\u2019d have a much different salary structure in the NBA, as there would be no rookie slotting for drafted players. And if you think the game\u2019s superstars would stand idly by and watch more of that cheddar that they helped produce go out the door to guys who haven\u2019t yet done anything \u2026 you\u2019d be wrong. So, the Draft isn\u2019t going anywhere. Which means the NBA must decide whether it wants to continue to be shocked, shocked that tanking is going on in its league, or accept the reality that there is not much patience for being in the middle ground in a league where every team is now worth more than $1 billion. There is only, as Pat Riley said a long time ago, winning and misery. * * * Longtime NBA reporter, columnist and Naismith Memorial Basketball Hall of Famer David Aldridge is an analyst for TNT. Smith mum on kind of soup thrown at coach","content":" INDEPENDENCE, Ohio (AP) \u2014 J.R. Smith\u2019s return from suspension was, ahem, soup-er. For one half. Smith scored all 19 of his points in the second half Saturday, but the struggling Cavaliers couldn\u2019t stop Denver\u2019s long-range shooting attack in a 126-117 loss to the Nuggets. Much like his season, Smith\u2019s comeback after he was benched by Cleveland for throwing a bowl of soup at Cavs assistant coach Damon Jones was strange. One of the NBA\u2019s least bashful shooters, Smith did not attempt a shot in the first half. But he let loose after halftime, making 7 of 8 shots before missing his last four. \u201cIn the second half I just figured to hell with it, just be more aggressive,\u201d Smith said. \u201cIf they double me, try to get around them and then make a play. Just a different mindset, I guess.\u201d It\u2019s been an interesting 72 hours for Smith, who did not reveal his soup choice or the ingredients that steamed him so much he threw his soup at Jones following Thursday\u2019s shootaround. He sat out that night\u2019s game against the 76ers, adding another dramatic twist to what has been a tumultuous season for the three-time defending Eastern Conference champions. Asked before the Cavs fell to the Nuggets what he could share about the soup spat, Smith was as cold as gazpacho. \u201cNothing,\u201d he said. And as for the kind of soup he flung? \u201cHonestly,\u201d he said Saturday following the team\u2019s shootaround. \u201cI don\u2019t even remember.\u201d Smith refused to say if he agreed with the discipline. He said he spoke with his teammates about what happened but not with Jones, a former NBA player who has been on Tyronn Lue\u2019s staff for two seasons. \u201cIt\u2019s not really my call,\u201d the 31-year-old said on the merits of his punishment. \u201cMore than anything, I talked to my teammates about it, everybody seemed cool. We moved on from it as a team, so whether it warranted a suspension or not, that\u2019s not my job. I\u2019m just here to play basketball.\u201d Because of Smith\u2019s volatile reputation and shoot-first-ask-questions later playing style, his soup-throwing tantrum led to a social-media storm Friday as Twitter filled with posts about him and his possible soup choice. Smith was amused \u2013 to a point. \u201cSome of them were actually pretty funny,\u201d he said. \u201cI understand that everything I do is going to have a meme or whatever behind it. That\u2019s just part of the day and age we live in. If this was 15 years ago, nobody would have even knew. It\u2019s just part of the game.\u201d Smith\u2019s had a challenging season on the floor \u2013 his 8.3 point average is his lowest since 2005 and he\u2019s making only 39 percent his shots \u2013 and his blowup at Jones could be the result of pent-up frustration. Smith, though, dodged a question about whether it was spur of the moment or something that had been building. \u201cI have an idea of what I want to say, but I\u2019m not going to say it,\u201d he said.\u201d So I\u2019m going to leave that as it is.\u201d Smith has been a key contributor to Cleveland reaching the NBA Finals the past three seasons.
